=== USER  (2026-07-07T13:45:43.563Z) ===
I run several python in parallel. python file1.py, python file2.py,.... Now some of them finish. How do I know which is finished ?

=== ASSISTANT  (2026-07-07T13:45:47.768Z) ===
[thinking]


=== ASSISTANT  (2026-07-07T13:45:56.791Z) ===
The cleanest way depends on how you launched them. Since you already started them with plain `python fileN.py`, here are your options:

## If they're still running in this shell/terminal

Use `jobs` to see which background jobs are still active:

```bash
jobs -l
```

Running jobs show `Running`; finished ones show `Done`. But this only works if you backgrounded them with `&` in the *same* shell, and `Done` entries disappear after you view them once.

## Check which processes are still alive

```bash
ps aux | grep '[p]ython'
```

Whatever `fileN.py` you **still see** is unfinished; the ones missing have finished (or crashed). The `[p]ython` trick keeps grep from matching itself.

Or more targeted:

```bash
pgrep -af 'python file'
```

## The reliable way — going forward

Plain parallel launches give you no record of *when* each finished or its exit status. Wrap each so it leaves a marker:

```bash
for f in file1 file2 file3; do
  (python $f.py > $f.log 2>&1; echo "DONE $f exit=$?" >> finished.log) &
done
```

Then just watch:

```bash
tail -f finished.log      # see each as it completes, with exit code
```

Any script listed in `finished.log` is done; the rest are still running. The per-file `.log` captures its output.

---

Which situation are you in — did you launch them with `&` in one terminal, or separately? If they're already running and you just need to know *now*, run the `pgrep -af 'python file'` above: the ones it **doesn't** list are the finished ones.

Want me to write a small script that watches your specific files and prints each one as it finishes?

Here's the full workflow.

## 1. Prepare the files (you do this)

```bash
cd /home/guoniu/cl/sunconj/public_html

# gzip the two oversized files
gzip -k output_frac_minus_1000.txt      # -k keeps the original too
gzip -k output_frac_plus_1000.txt
ls -lh output_frac_*_1000.txt.gz         # confirm each is < 100 MB
```

After you've compared and deleted the `.txttt` files, and confirmed the `.gz` files are small enough, you decide whether to upload the original `.txt` or only the `.gz`. **Don't upload both** the 112 MB `.txt` and its `.gz` — put only the `.gz` in the repo (add a note in the README that reviewers run `gunzip`).

## 2. Create the GitHub repo

The key point: this folder is currently tied to your **GitLab** repo. You want a **fresh, standalone** repo for the journal — don't reuse this git history. Easiest is to make a clean copy:

```bash
mkdir ~/sunconj-github
cd ~/sunconj-github
# copy only what you want public — adjust the list:

cp /home/guoniu/cl/sunconj/public_html/sunconj.pdf .

# ... etc, pick the files the journal needs

git init
git add -A
git commit -m "Supplementary data and code for sunconj paper"
```

## 3. Push to GitHub

**If you have the `gh` CLI** (simplest — creates the repo and pushes in one step):

```bash
gh auth login          # once, if not already logged in
gh repo create sunconj --public --source=. --push
```

**Or via the website:**
1. Go to github.com → **New repository** → name it (e.g. `sunconj`) → **don't** add a README → Create.
2. Then:
```bash
git remote add origin git@github.com:YOURUSERNAME/sunconj.git
git branch -M main
git push -u origin main
```

The journal then gets the URL `https://github.com/YOURUSERNAME/sunconj`.

=== ASSISTANT  (2026-07-07T14:13:36.103Z) ===
That's a sensible choice — for a one-time journal upload, the web version is the least hassle. No git commands, no `gh` CLI. But there's **one important limit to know about**:

> **The GitHub web uploader caps each file at 25 MB** (that's stricter than the 100 MB git-push limit). Files over 25 MB simply can't be dragged into the browser.

So check your gzipped files first:

```bash
ls -lh output_frac_*_1000.txt.gz
```

- If each `.gz` is **under 25 MB** → you're fine, upload them via the web.
- If still **over 25 MB** → either upload via git command line instead, or split/trim them (or ask whether the journal really needs those giant output dumps).

## Web upload steps

1. Go to **github.com** → sign in → click **+** (top right) → **New repository**.
2. Name it (e.g. `sunconj`), set **Public**, **check "Add a README"**, click **Create repository**.
3. On the repo page, click **Add file** → **Upload files**.
4. **Drag and drop** your files (or click "choose your files"). You can select many at once.
5. Scroll down, type a short commit message, click **Commit changes**.

That's it — the files are live at `https://github.com/YOURNAME/sunconj`, which is the URL you give the journal.

## A couple of tips
- **Skip the `.txttt` files and the raw oversized `.txt`** — upload only the files you actually want public (the `.py` scripts, `sunconj.pdf`, the `data_*.txt`, and the small-enough `.gz`).
- You can upload in **several batches** — just do "Add file → Upload files" again for more. Handy if you want folders or want to keep batches organized.
- The web uploader **can't create empty folders**, but you can type `foldername/` at the start of the filename field when uploading, or just keep everything flat.

Do you want me to check the gzipped file sizes for you (read-only, I won't change anything), so you know before you start whether they clear the 25 MB web limit? If they're too big, I can suggest the smallest-effort fix.

35	\title[A constructive approach to unit fractions]{A constructive approach to unit fractions with shifted prime denominators}
36	
37	\author{Guo-Niu HAN}
38	\address{I.R.M.A., UMR 7501, Universit\'e de Strasbourg
39	et CNRS, 7 rue Ren\'e Descartes, F-67084 Strasbourg, France}
40	\email{guoniu.han@unistra.fr}
41	
42	
43	\subjclass[2020]{11A67, 11D68, 11Y16}
44	
45	\keywords{ Egyptian fractions, unit fractions, prime numbers,
46	Sun's conjecture, beam search, greedy algorithm}
47	
48	\date{May 4, 2026}
49	
50	\begin{document}
51	
52	\maketitle
53	
54	
55	
56	
57	\begin{abstract}
58	A conjecture of Zhi-Wei Sun states that every positive rational number
59	can be written as a finite sum of unit fractions whose denominators are
60	of the form $p+1$ or $p-1$, where $p$ runs over distinct primes.
61	Both cases have recently been proved in full generality by
62	Thomas Bloom~\cite{Bloom}.
63	However, even for small integers such as $r=2$ or $r=3$,
64	finding an explicit decomposition is highly non-trivial: the required
65	primes can have hundreds of decimal digits, and the construction
66	involves hundreds of elimination steps.
67	We exhibit explicit decompositions for these two integer cases:
68	the case $r=2$ with denominators $p+1$, using a set
69	of $1963$ distinct primes, and the case $r=3$ with denominators
70	$p-1$, using a set of $698$ distinct primes.
71	The algorithm behind these constructions --- based on beam search
72	and splitting tables --- may be of independent interest for related
73	problems.
74	\end{abstract}
75	
76	
77	
78	% -------------------------------------------------------------------
79	\section{Introduction}
80	% -------------------------------------------------------------------
81	
82	The representation of a positive rational as a sum of distinct unit fractions
83	$1/n$ is a classical topic going back to Egyptian fraction expansions~\cite{ES,Graham}.
84	A more recent variant restricts the denominators to lie in arithmetic
85	sets of the form $\{p+s : p \text{ prime}\}$ for a fixed integer shift $s$.
86	Two natural choices are $s = +1$ and $s = -1$, which lead to the
87	following results, initially conjectured by Sun~\cite{Sun} and recently
88	proved by Thomas Bloom~\cite{Bloom}.
89	
90	\begin{theorem}[Bloom~\cite{Bloom}]\label{conj:sun_plus}
91	For every positive rational number $r$, there exists a finite set $P$
92	of prime numbers such that
93	\[
94	  r \;=\; \sum_{p \in P} \frac{1}{p+1}.
95	\]
96	\end{theorem}
97	
98	\begin{theorem}[Bloom~\cite{Bloom}]\label{conj:sun_minus}
99	For every positive rational number $r$, there exists a finite set $P$
100	of prime numbers such that
101	\[
102	  r \;=\; \sum_{p \in P} \frac{1}{p-1}.
103	\]
104	\end{theorem}
105	
106	Sun had verified these statements for many small rationals.
107	Qing-Hu Hou has verified them for all rational
108	numbers $r \in (0,1)$ with denominator not exceeding $100$~\cite{Sun}.
109	However, even for small integers such as $r=2$ or $r=3$,
110	finding an explicit decomposition is highly non-trivial: the required
111	primes can have hundreds of decimal digits, and the construction
112	involves hundreds of elimination steps.
113	
114	The present paper provides such explicit constructions for the two
115	integer cases $r=2$ (shift $s=+1$) and $r=3$ (shift $s=-1$),
116	together with a practical algorithm that may serve as a starting point
117	for related problems.
118	
119	\begin{theorem}[Case $r = 2$, $s = +1$]\label{thm:main_plus}
120	There exists a set $P_+$ of $1963$ distinct prime numbers, the largest of
121	which has $181$ decimal digits and equals
122	\begin{center}
123	{\begin{minipage}{0.75\textwidth}
124	\normalfont\small\seqsplit{2361944081811262661581420029993779369004749258112890856900708493649883531810055173157404200152116544006497944823707101618004503106964633107234444169742408558510043346214786314674399}
125	\end{minipage}}
126	\end{center}
127	such that
128	\[
129	  \sum_{p \in P_+} \frac{1}{p+1} \;=\; 2.
130	\]
131	\end{theorem}
132	
133	\begin{theorem}[Case $r = 3$, $s = -1$]\label{thm:main_minus}
134	There exists a set $P_-$ of $698$ distinct prime numbers, the largest of
135	which has $68$ decimal digits and equals
136	\[
137	21405811313494409045983653494328316787629317190709732873740497324001,
138	\]
139	such that
140	\[
141	  \sum_{p \in P_-} \frac{1}{p-1} \;=\; 3.
142	\]
143	\end{theorem}
144	
145	The same algorithm, run on a wide range of inputs, also establishes
146	the following further verifications.
147	
148	\begin{theorem}[Further verified cases]\label{thm:families}
149	For each shift $s \in \{+1, -1\}$, Theorems~\ref{conj:sun_plus}
150	and~\ref{conj:sun_minus} hold for every rational $r$ in the following
151	families:
152	\begin{enumerate}
153	\item all reduced fractions $a/b$ with $1 \leq a < b \leq 1000$;
154	\item all reciprocals $1/p$ for primes $p \leq 100\,000$;
155	\item all reciprocals $1/2^k$ for integers $1 \leq k \leq 40$.
156	\end{enumerate}
157	In each case an explicit set $P$ of distinct primes with
158	$\sum_{p \in P} 1/(p+s) = r$ is produced and verified in exact rational
159	arithmetic.
160	\end{theorem}
161	
162	The proofs are constructive and use exactly the same algorithm, run
163	with different inputs: the sets $P_+$ and $P_-$ are produced by the
164	program \texttt{sunconj.py} described in Section~\ref{sec:algo} and
165	listed in full in the supplementary files \texttt{prime\_set\_P\_plus2.txt}
166	and \texttt{prime\_set\_P\_minus3.txt}. All identities are verified
167	in arbitrary-precision rational arithmetic (\texttt{gmpy2.mpq}).
168	
169	\smallskip
170	
171	{\it Historical remark.}
172	The constructions and the program described in this paper were
173	completed in 2018. The paper was not submitted
174	at that time for personal reasons. It is presented here for the
175	explicit prime sets and the algorithmic contribution.
176	
177	
178	All programs and data files are available on the author's personal
179	webpage, allowing independent verification of the computations:
180	\begin{center}
181	\tt https://irma.math.unistra.fr/\char126 guoniu/sunconj.html
182	\end{center}
183	
184	The paper is organised as follows.
185	Section~\ref{sec:notation} introduces notation.
186	Section~\ref{sec:algo} describes the algorithm, with worked examples.
187	Section~\ref{sec:example} illustrates the first three iterations on
188	the run $(r=2,s=+1)$.
189	Section~\ref{sec:proof} gives the proofs of
190	Theorems~\ref{thm:main_plus} and~\ref{thm:main_minus}.
191	
192	
193	% -------------------------------------------------------------------
194	\section{Notation and setting}\label{sec:notation}
195	% -------------------------------------------------------------------
196	
197	We denote by $\PP$ the set of primes. Throughout the paper, $s$ denotes
198	a fixed shift in $\{+1, -1\}$. For a finite multiset $M$ of primes
199	we write
200	\[
201	  S(M) \;=\; \sum_{p \in M} \frac{1}{p+s}
202	\]
203	(counting multiplicities). When the shift needs to be displayed
204	explicitly we write $S^{(s)}(M)$. A \emph{prime decomposition} of a
205	positive rational $r$ at shift $s$ is a finite \emph{set}
206	$P \subset \PP$  such that
207	$S^{(s)}(P) = r$. 
208	
209	A central elementary identity for our algorithm is
210	\begin{equation}\label{eq:split}
211	  \frac{1}{p+s} \;=\; \frac{1}{p+s+1} \;+\; \frac{1}{(p+s)(p+s+1)},
212	\end{equation}
213	which splits one term $1/(p+s)$ into two terms with strictly larger
214	denominators. More generally, for a prime $p$ and a positive integer $m$,
215	one is led to the following sub-problem:
216	
217	\begin{quote}
218	\textit{Find a finite set $Q$ of primes with $\min Q > p$ such that}
219	\[
220	  \frac{m}{p+s} \;=\; \sum_{q \in Q} \frac{1}{q+s}.
221	\]
222	\end{quote}
223	
224	We call such a $Q$ a \emph{prime replacement} for the triple $(p,m,s)$. The
225	existence of prime replacements for every $(p,m)$ would imply
226	Theorems~\ref{conj:sun_plus} and~\ref{conj:sun_minus} constructively for all
227	positive rationals. In our algorithm we do not solve this sub-problem in full
228	generality. Instead, we solve it heuristically for the finitely many triples
229	$(p,m,s)$ that arise during a run, and we verify each replacement in exact
230	arithmetic.
231	\medskip
232	
233	A crucial concept of our approach is the \emph{excess count} of a
234	multiset. For a finite multiset $M$ of primes we define
235	\[
236	  \ct(M) \;=\; \sum_{p \in M, \, \mu_M(p) \geq 2} \bigl(\mu_M(p) - 1\bigr),
237	\]
238	where $\mu_M(p)$ is the multiplicity of $p$ in $M$. Thus $\ct(M)=0$
239	if and only if $M$ is a set. The whole point of Phase~2 of the algorithm
240	is to drive $\ct$ down to $0$.
241	
242	
243	% -------------------------------------------------------------------
244	\section{The algorithm}\label{sec:algo}
245	% -------------------------------------------------------------------
246	
247	The algorithm is implemented in the Python program \texttt{sunconj.py},
248	which uses \texttt{gmpy2}~\cite{gmpy2} for exact rational arithmetic and primality
249	testing. The shift $s \in \{+1, -1\}$ is a parameter; on the cases
250	treated here, the program runs in roughly $6$ minutes on a standard
251	desktop computer for $(r,s) = (2,+1)$, and in well under a minute for
252	$(r,s) = (3,-1)$.
253	
254	\subsection{Outline}
255	
256	The algorithm has two phases.
257	\begin{description}
258	\item[Phase~1.] \emph{Initial multiset.}
259	  Find a multiset $M_0$ of primes with $S^{(s)}(M_0) = r$.
260	\item[Phase~2.] \emph{Deduplication.}
261	  Iteratively replace one repeated prime at a time, until the multiset
262	  becomes a set.
263	\end{description}
264	Both phases share a common subroutine, the \emph{beam search} of
265	Section~\ref{sec:beam}, which produces decompositions of a rational
266	$r$ as a sum of distinct unit fractions $1/(p+s)$ with $p$ prime
267	and $p$ above a prescribed lower bound.
268	
269	\subsection{Splitting tables}\label{sec:tables}
270	
271	The beam search is guided by a precomputed list of partitions of $1$
272	into two or three reduced fractions; we call this list a
273	\emph{splitting table}. Concretely, we use:
274	\begin{itemize}
275	\item all pairs $(a, 1-a)$ with $a$ a reduced fraction $j/k$,
276	  $1 \leq j < k$, denominator $k$ below a small bound;
277	\item all triples $(a, b, 1-a-b)$, ordered $a < b < 1-a-b$, with
278	  the same denominator constraint;
279	\item the trivial table entry $[1]$.
280	\end{itemize}
281	Three tables are built, of increasing size and thoroughness:
282	\textsf{Fast} (denominators $<10$ for pairs, $<7$ for triples),
283	\textsf{Middle} ($<20$ and $<13$),
284	\textsf{Deep} ($<30$ and $<17$).
285	
286	Given a target rational $r$ and a table entry $[w_1,\ldots,w_t]$
287	(with $w_1+\cdots+w_t=1$), the algorithm replaces the single problem
288	``decompose $r$'' by the $t$ independent sub-problems
289	``decompose $w_i r$'' ($i=1,\ldots,t$), each handled by a separate
290	call to the beam search, since
291	$$w_1 r+\cdots+w_t r=r.$$
292	%
293	Since $w_i <1$, the sub-problems are ``easier'' because each
294	$w_i r$ is strictly smaller than $r$, so the greedy search reaches
295	zero in fewer steps.
296	
297	This trick is essential: a direct greedy expansion of a generic
298	rational $r$ tends to produce extremely long sequences of primes,
299	or to fail to terminate within reasonable cutoffs,
300	whereas after splitting via a table entry the individual
301	sub-rationals are typically solvable in a few steps.
302	
303	
304	The \textsf{Fast} table contains $29$ entries: the trivial entry $[1]$,
305	the $27$ ordered pairs $(j/k,\, 1-j/k)$ with $\gcd(j,k)=1$,
306	$1 \leq j < k < 10$, and the single triple with denominator below~$7$:
307	\[
308	  \frac{1}{6} + \frac{1}{3} + \frac{1}{2} \;=\; 1.
309	\]
310	The first few pairs, in the order they appear in the table, are
311	\[
312	  \frac{1}{2}+\frac{1}{2},\quad
313	  \frac{1}{3}+\frac{2}{3},\quad
314	  \frac{1}{4}+\frac{3}{4},\quad
315	  \frac{1}{5}+\frac{4}{5},\quad
316	  \frac{2}{5}+\frac{3}{5},\quad
317	  \frac{1}{6}+\frac{5}{6},\quad\ldots
318	\]
319	
320	
321	See Examples~\ref{ex:split} and \ref{ex:phase1} for illustrations of how the splitting tables work.
322	
323	\subsection{Beam search}\label{sec:beam}
324	
325	The beam search is the core decomposer. Given a rational $r > 0$ and
326	a lower bound $b$, it seeks a list of distinct primes
327	$b< p_1 < p_2 < \cdots < p_k$ 
328	with 
329	$\sum 1/(p_i+s) = r$. It proceeds as follows.
330	
331	\begin{enumerate}
332	\item \emph{Candidate generation.} For each rational
333	  remainder encountered, list the $n$ smallest primes
334	  $p > b$ with $1/(p+s) \leq r$. The width $n$ is $3$, $4$, or $6$
335	  in modes \textsf{Fast}, \textsf{Middle}, \textsf{Deep} respectively.
336	
337	
338	\item \emph{Beam expansion.} The \emph{frontier} (the current set of
339	  partial decompositions being explored, each recorded as a pair
340	  $[\text{primes chosen so far},\ \text{remaining sum}]$) is
341	  expanded for $1$ round (mode \textsf{Fast}) or $3$ rounds
342	  (\textsf{Middle}/\textsf{Deep}), each round multiplying the frontier
343	  size by $n$.
344	
345	\item \emph{Greedy completion.} From every leaf of the beam, a pure
346	  greedy completion is attempted: at each step one subtracts the
347	  largest feasible $1/(p+s)$ until the remainder reaches~$0$.
348	  If the remainder has not reached $0$ after $99$ primes have
349	  been selected, the completion returns \textsc{False} and the
350	  leaf is discarded.
351	  There is no prime-size cutoff: when the unsolved remainder
352	  is a very small rational, the beam candidates are necessarily
353	  huge primes, and the beam expansion (Step~2) returns the
354	  smallest one with no restriction on its size. This is how
355	  primes with hundreds of digits appear in the final set~$P$.
356	
357	\item \emph{Selection.} Among all successful completions, the one
358	  minimising the sum of primes is returned.
359	\end{enumerate}
360	
361	\begin{example}
362	Beam search on target $r = 1/3$, lower bound $b=1$,
363		mode \textsf{Fast}.
364	
365	
366	\textit{Step~1: candidate generation.}
367	List the $n=3$ smallest primes $p > b$ with $1/(p+1) \leq 1/3$,
368	i.e.\ $p \geq 2$:
369	\[
370	  p = 2 \;\bigl({\textstyle\frac{1}{3}}\bigr), \quad
371	  p = 3 \;\bigl({\textstyle\frac{1}{4}}\bigr), \quad
372	  p = 5 \;\bigl({\textstyle\frac{1}{6}}\bigr).
373	\]
374	
375	\textit{Step~2: beam expansion (1 round).}
376	Branch on each candidate and record the remainder:
377	\[
378	\begin{array}{lll}
379	  \text{branch } p=2: & \text{take } \tfrac{1}{3}, &
380	    \text{remainder } = \tfrac{1}{3} - \tfrac{1}{3} = 0
381	    \quad\Rightarrow\quad \text{already complete},\\[4pt]
382	  \text{branch } p=3: & \text{take } \tfrac{1}{4}, &
383	    \text{remainder } = \tfrac{1}{3} - \tfrac{1}{4} = \tfrac{1}{12},\\[4pt]
384	  \text{branch } p=5: & \text{take } \tfrac{1}{6}, &
385	    \text{remainder } = \tfrac{1}{3} - \tfrac{1}{6} = \tfrac{1}{6}.
386	\end{array}
387	\]
388	
389	\textit{Step~3: greedy completion of each leaf.}
390	\begin{itemize}
391	\item Leaf $[2]$: remainder $0$, done. \quad Result: $\{2\}$.
392	\item Leaf $[3]$: remainder $\tfrac{1}{12}$.
393	  Smallest prime $p > 3$ with $\tfrac{1}{p+1} \leq \tfrac{1}{12}$
394	  is $p = 11$; taking it leaves remainder $0$.
395	  Result: $\{3, 11\}$.
396	\item Leaf $[5]$: remainder $\tfrac{1}{6}$.
397	  Take $p=7$ ($\tfrac{1}{8}$), remainder $\tfrac{1}{6}-\tfrac{1}{8}=\tfrac{1}{24}$;
398	  then $p=23$ ($\tfrac{1}{24}$), remainder $0$.
399	  Result: $\{5, 7, 23\}$.
400	\end{itemize}
401	
402	\textit{Step~4: selection.}
403	Among the three completions, the one minimising the sum of primes is chosen:
404	\[
405	  \{2\} \;(\text{sum }= 2), \quad
406	  \{3,11\} \;(\text{sum }= 14), \quad
407	  \{5,7,23\} \;(\text{sum }= 35).
408	\]
409	The beam search returns $\{2\}$, and indeed $\tfrac{1}{2+1} = \tfrac{1}{3}$.
410	Note that all three sets are valid decompositions of $\tfrac{1}{3}$;
411	the beam search finds all of them and picks the simplest.
412	\end{example}
413	
414	\begin{example}[Beam search on target $r = 1/2$, lower bound $b = 6$,
415		mode \textsf{Fast}]
416	This is the sub-problem arising in iteration~3 of Phase~2
417	(elimination of $p^* = 5$, $m = 4$, target $(m-1)/(p^*+1) = 1/2$).
418	The lower bound $b = p^*+1 = 6$ ensures that all replacement primes are strictly greater than $5$.
419	
420	\textit{Step~1: candidate generation.}
421	The $n=3$ smallest primes $p > 5$ with $1/(p+1) \leq 1/2$ are:
422	\[
423	  p = 7 \;\bigl({\textstyle\frac{1}{8}}\bigr), \quad
424	  p = 11 \;\bigl({\textstyle\frac{1}{12}}\bigr), \quad
425	  p = 13 \;\bigl({\textstyle\frac{1}{14}}\bigr).
426	\]
427	
428	\textit{Step~2: beam expansion (1 round).}
429	\[
430	\begin{array}{lll}
431	  \text{branch } p=7{:}  & \text{take } \tfrac{1}{8},  &
432	    \text{remainder} = \tfrac{1}{2}-\tfrac{1}{8} = \tfrac{3}{8},\\[4pt]
433	  \text{branch } p=11{:} & \text{take } \tfrac{1}{12}, &
434	    \text{remainder} = \tfrac{1}{2}-\tfrac{1}{12} = \tfrac{5}{12},\\[4pt]
435	  \text{branch } p=13{:} & \text{take } \tfrac{1}{14}, &
436	    \text{remainder} = \tfrac{1}{2}-\tfrac{1}{14} = \tfrac{3}{7}.
437	\end{array}
438	\]
439	
440	\textit{Step~3: greedy completion of each leaf.}
441	\begin{itemize}
442	\item Leaf $[7]$, remainder $\frac{3}{8}$:
443	  take $p=11$ ($\frac{1}{12}$, rem.\ $\frac{7}{24}$),
444	  $p=13$ ($\frac{1}{14}$, rem.\ $\frac{37}{168}$),
445	  $\ldots$, eventually reaching $p = 2{,}169{,}227$.
446	  Result: $\{7\cm 11\cm 13\cm 17\cm 19\cm 23\cm 29\cm 31\cm 127\cm 1613\cm 2169227\}$,\quad sum of primes $\approx 2.17\times 10^6$.
447	\item Leaf $[11]$, remainder $\frac{5}{12}$:
448	  take $p=13\cm 17\cm 19\cm 23\cm 29\cm 31\cm 37\cm 41\cm 43\cm 47$.
449	  Result: $\{11\cm 13\cm 17\cm 19\cm 23\cm 29\cm 31\cm 37\cm 41\cm 43\cm 47\}$,\quad sum $= 311$.
450	\item Leaf $[13]$, remainder $\frac{3}{7}$:
451	  take $p=17\cm 19\cm 23\cm 29\cm 31\cm 37\cm 41\cm 43\cm 47\cm 53$.
452	  Result: $\{13\cm 17\cm 19\cm 23\cm 29\cm 31\cm 37\cm 41\cm 43\cm 47\cm 53\}$,\quad sum $= 353$.
453	\end{itemize}
454	
455	\textit{Step~4: selection.}
456	The beam selects the completion with the smallest sum of primes,
457	namely leaf $[11]$ with sum $311$.
458	The beam search therefore returns
459	$Q = \{11\cm 13\cm 17\cm 19\cm 23\cm 29\cm 31\cm 37\cm 41\cm 43\cm 47\}$, and indeed
460	\[
461	  \sum_{p \in Q} \frac{1}{p+1}
462	  = \frac{1}{12}+\frac{1}{14}+\frac{1}{18}+\frac{1}{20}+\frac{1}{24}
463	  +\frac{1}{30}+\frac{1}{32}+\frac{1}{38}+\frac{1}{42}+\frac{1}{44}+\frac{1}{48}
464	  = \frac{1}{2}.
465	\]
466	Comparing the three leaves, leaf $[7]$ produces a prime with more than
467	six decimal digits — illustrating why a pure greedy search (always
468	taking the largest possible term) is impractical.
469	The beam search, by exploring multiple branches simultaneously, finds
470	the much cleaner solution from leaf $[11]$.
471	\end{example}
472	
473	\begin{example}
474	[Splitting table + beam search, target $r = 1/2$,
475		lower bound $b = 6$, mode \textsf{Middle}]
476		\label{ex:split}
477	
478	This is the sub-problem arising in iteration~3 of Phase~2
479	(elimination of $p^* = 5$, $m = 4$, replacement target
480	$(m-1)/(p^*+1) = 3/6 = 1/2$, lower bound $b = p^*+1 = 6$).
481	The actual run uses mode \textsf{Middle} (since \texttt{prefer\_large=True}).
482	
483	
484	The function \texttt{\_decompose\_via\_split\_table} iterates through
485	the \textsf{Middle} table ($82$ entries) until one fully succeeds.
486	In our example, the first successful entry is Entry~$1$,
487	the pair $[1/4,\,3/4]$, which splits the target $1/2$ into two
488	independent sub-problems:
489	\[
490	  \frac{1}{4}\cdot\frac{1}{2} \;=\; \frac{1}{8},
491	  \qquad
492	  \frac{3}{4}\cdot\frac{1}{2} \;=\; \frac{3}{8}.
493	\]
494	
495	\textit{Beam on $1/8$, lower bound $6$.}
496	The unique prime $p > 5$ with $1/(p+1) = 1/8$ is $p = 7$.
497	The beam returns $\{7\}$ immediately.
498	
499	\textit{Beam on $3/8$, lower bound $6$.}
500	The beam (with $n=4$ candidates in \textsf{Middle} mode, and $3$
501	expansion rounds) returns $\{7, 11, 19, 23, 29, 31, 97, 4703\}$.
502	Indeed,
503	\[
504	  \frac{1}{8}+\frac{1}{12}+\frac{1}{20}+\frac{1}{24}
505	  +\frac{1}{30}+\frac{1}{32}+\frac{1}{98}+\frac{1}{4704}
506	  \;=\; \frac{3}{8}.
507	\]
508	
509	\textit{Combined result.}
510	Concatenating the two sub-results gives
511	$Q = \{7\cm 7\cm 11\cm 19\cm 23\cm 29\cm 31\cm 97\cm 4703\}$, and indeed
512	\[
513	  \frac{1}{8}+\frac{1}{8}+\frac{1}{12}+\frac{1}{20}+\frac{1}{24}
514	  +\frac{1}{30}+\frac{1}{32}+\frac{1}{98}+\frac{1}{4704}
515	  \;=\; \frac{1}{2}.
516	\]
517	The prime $7$ appears twice because both sub-problems independently
518	selected it as their first term.
519	This is consistent with what was reported in iteration~3: the two
520	sub-problems are solved in isolation, without any global check on
521	which primes are already present, so new multiplicities can be
522	introduced — which is precisely why $\ct$ rises to $9$ after
523	this iteration.
524	
525	\end{example}
526	
527	\subsection{Phase~1: initial multiset $M_0$}\label{sec:phase1}
528	
529	In Phase~1 we apply the beam search above to the target rational $r$
530	in mode \textsf{Fast} (then \textsf{Middle}, then \textsf{Deep} on
531	failure). 
532	
533	\begin{example}[Phase~1 for the run $(r=2,\,s=+1)$]\label{ex:phase1}
534	The beam search is called on target $r=2$ in mode \textsf{Fast}.
535	The function \texttt{\_decompose\_via\_split\_table} tries table
536	entries in order; the first one that fully succeeds is the pair
537	$[2/5,\,3/5]$, which splits the target into two independent
538	sub-problems:
539	\[
540	  \frac{2}{5}\cdot 2 \;=\; \frac{4}{5},
541	  \qquad
542	  \frac{3}{5}\cdot 2 \;=\; \frac{6}{5}.
543	\]
544	The beam solves each independently:
545	\[
546	  \frac{4}{5} \;\longrightarrow\; \{2,3,5,19\}, \qquad
547	  \frac{6}{5} \;\longrightarrow\; \{2,3,5,7,11,13,17,19,23,43,3467,4005539\}.
548	\]
549	Concatenating the two sub-results gives the initial multiset
550	\[
551	  M_0^+ \;=\; \{\,2,2,3,3,5,5,7,11,13,17,19,19,23,43,3467,4005539\,\},
552	\]
553	of size $16$, with $\ct(M_0^+)=4$. The four repeated primes are
554	$2$, $3$, $5$, and $19$, each appearing exactly twice, one copy
555	coming from each sub-result. One verifies in exact rational arithmetic that
556	\[
557	  \sum_{p\,\in\,M_0^+} \frac{1}{p+1}
558	  \;=\;
559	  \frac{2}{3}+\frac{2}{4}+\frac{2}{6}
560	  +\frac{1}{8}+\frac{1}{12}+\frac{1}{14}+\frac{1}{18}
561	  +\frac{2}{20}+\frac{1}{24}+\frac{1}{44}
562	  +\frac{1}{3468}+\frac{1}{4005540}
563	  \;=\; 2.
564	\]
565	Phase~1 terminates here and hands $M_0^+$ to Phase~2,
566	which will iteratively eliminate the four repeated primes.
567	\end{example}
568	
569	\subsection{Phase~2: deduplication loop}\label{sec:phase2}
570	
571	Let $M$ denote the current multiset, initialised to $M = M_0$. Each
572	iteration of Phase~2 performs the following step.
573	
574	\begin{enumerate}
575	\item \emph{Pick a repeated prime.} Let $p^* = \min\{p \in M : \mu_M(p) \geq 2\}$
576	  and let $m = \mu_M(p^*)$.
577	\item \emph{Replace.} Find a list $Q$ of distinct primes, all $> p^*$,
578	  with 
579	  \[
580	    \sum_{q \in Q} \frac{1}{q+s} = \frac{m-1}{p^*+s}.
581	  \]
582	  This is done by calling the beam search of Section~\ref{sec:beam}
583	  on the target $(m-1)/(p^*+s)$ with lower bound $p^*$, with each
584	  splitting table entry tried in turn.
585	\item \emph{Update.} Remove $m-1$ copies of $p^*$ from $M$, and
586	  add $Q$. The new multiset $M'$ satisfies
587	  $S^{(s)}(M') = S^{(s)}(M) = r$.
588	\end{enumerate}
589	
590	See Section~\ref{sec:example} for an illustration of how this works.
591	
592	\subsection{Termination}\label{sec:traj}
593	
594	
595	A naive expectation is that $\ct$ should decrease monotonically.
596	This is \emph{not} the case: the replacement of $m-1$ copies of $p^*$
597	by a list $Q$ of new primes may introduce \emph{new} multiplicities,
598	because some primes in $Q$ may already lie in $M$. Thus $\ct$ can rise
599	sharply in the early iterations.
600	
601	As shown in the two figures of Section~\ref{sec:proof}, the trajectory
602	of $\ct$ in practice follows a roughly unimodal shape: it first rises
603	(with occasional downward fluctuations) to a peak, then decreases
604	(with occasional upward fluctuations) to $0$. This behaviour is not
605	proved mathematically, but can be intuitively understood as follows.
606	In the early iterations, the repeated primes are small and their
607	replacements $Q$ frequently collide with primes already in $M$,
608	causing $\ct$ to grow. As the frontier of unresolved primes moves
609	upward, collisions become rarer and $\ct$ begins its descent.
610	
611	The algorithm terminates successfully if and only if $\ct$ eventually
612	reaches $0$. Guaranteeing termination for all inputs $r$ would provide
613	a fully constructive proof of Theorems~\ref{conj:sun_plus}
614	and~\ref{conj:sun_minus}, complementing Bloom's existential argument.
615	This remains open: we can only verify termination experimentally for
616	specific inputs, as reported in Section~\ref{sec:proof}.
617	
618	
619	
620	\section{Concrete examples from the run $(r=2,\,s=+1)$}\label{sec:example}
621	From Example~\ref{ex:phase1},
622	the initial multiset is
623	\[
624	M_0^+ = \{\,2,2,3,3,5,5,7,11,13,17,19,19,23,43,3467,4005539\,\},
625	\]
626	with $\ct(M_0^+)=4$, the four repeated primes being $2,3,5,19$.
627	
628	\medskip
629	\noindent\textit{Iteration 1: $p^*=2$, $m=2$.}
630	Phase~2 removes one copy of $2$ and must find a set $Q$
631	of distinct primes $>2$ satisfying
632	\[
633	  \sum_{q\in Q}\frac{1}{q+1} \;=\; \frac{m-1}{p^*+1} \;=\; \frac{1}{3}.
634	\]
635	The beam search, using the table entry $[1/3,\,2/3]$ to split the
636	target $1/3$ into sub-targets $1/9$ and $2/9$, returns
637	$Q=\{5,7,23\}$.  Indeed,
638	\[
639	  \frac{1}{6}+\frac{1}{8}+\frac{1}{24} \;=\; \frac{1}{3}.
640	\]
641	The new multiset is
642	\[
643	  M_1 = \{\,2,3,3,5,5,5,7,7,11,13,17,19,19,23,23,43,3467,4005539\,\}.
644	\]
645	Since $5$, $7$, $23$ were already in $M_0^+$, their multiplicities
646	increase, and $\ct$ rises from $4$ to $6$.
647	The repeated primes are now $\{3,5,7,19,23\}$
648	with multiplicities $\{2,3,2,2,2\}$.
649	
650	\medskip
651	\noindent\textit{Iteration 2: $p^*=3$, $m=2$.}
652	One copy of $3$ is removed; the replacement target is
653	\[
654	  \frac{m-1}{p^*+1} \;=\; \frac{1}{4}.
655	\]
656	The beam search returns $Q=\{5,11\}$. Indeed,
657	\[
658	  \frac{1}{6}+\frac{1}{12} \;=\; \frac{1}{4}.
659	\]
660	The new multiset is
661	\[
662	  M_2 = \{\,2,3,5,5,5,5,7,7,11,11,13,17,19,19,23,23,43,3467,4005539\,\}.
663	\]
664	Adding $5$ and $11$ (both already present) raises $\ct$ further to $7$.
665	The prime $5$ now has multiplicity $4$.
666	
667	\medskip
668	\noindent\textit{Iteration 3: $p^*=5$, $m=4$.}
669	The multiplicity of $5$ grew from $2$ in $M_0^+$ to $4$ in $M_2$
670	because iterations~1 and~2 each introduced an extra copy of $5$.
671	Three copies of $5$ are removed, leaving one.
672	The replacement target is
673	\[
674	  \frac{m-1}{p^*+1} \;=\; \frac{3}{6} \;=\; \frac{1}{2}.
675	\]
676	A larger target is not a problem: it simply means the beam search
677	must find more primes to fill it.
678	From Example~\ref{ex:split}, the beam search returns the multiset
679	$Q=\{7\cm 7\cm 11\cm 19\cm 23\cm 29\cm 31\cm 97\cm 4703\}$
680	(nine primes, with $7$ appearing twice). Indeed,
681	\[
682	  \frac{1}{8}+\frac{1}{8}+\frac{1}{12}+\frac{1}{20}+\frac{1}{24}
683	  +\frac{1}{30}+\frac{1}{32}+\frac{1}{98}+\frac{1}{4704}
684	  \;=\; \frac{1}{2}.
685	\]
686	Since five of the nine primes in $Q$ (namely $7,7,11,19,23$)
687	are already present in $M_2$, the count rises further:
688	$\ct(M_3) = 9$.
689	The repeated primes in $M_3$ are $\{7,11,19,23\}$
690	with multiplicities $\{4,3,3,3\}$.
691	
692	\medskip
693	This illustrates why $\ct$ grows during the early iterations:
694	each replacement introduces primes that are already in the multiset,
695	creating new collisions faster than old ones are resolved.
696	Although $\ct$ has increased at every step so far, the algorithm
697	is making irreversible progress:
698	the primes $2$, $3$, and $5$ will never be repeated again in any
699	subsequent multiset $M_j$. The frontier of unresolved primes moves
700	strictly upward at every iteration.
701	
702	
703	
704	
705	% -------------------------------------------------------------------
706	\section{The two runs: proofs of Theorems~\ref{thm:main_plus} and~\ref{thm:main_minus}}\label{sec:proof}
707	% -------------------------------------------------------------------
708	
709	The two theorems are obtained by running \texttt{sunconj.py} with
710	different command-line arguments. We summarise both runs in
711	Table~\ref{tab:summary}, then comment on the trajectory of $\ct$ in
712	each case.
713	
714	\begin{table}[h]
715	\centering
716	\begin{tabular}{l c c}
717	\hline
718	& Theorem~\ref{thm:main_plus}: $r=2$, $s=+1$
719	& Theorem~\ref{thm:main_minus}: $r=3$, $s=-1$ \\
720	\hline
721	Command          & \texttt{sunconj.py +1 2 false true} & \texttt{sunconj.py -1 3 false true} \\
722	$|M_0|$          & $16$                & $12$ \\
723	$\ct(M_0)$       & $4$                 & $2$ \\
724	Phase~2 iterations & $452$             & $167$ \\
725	Peak $\ct$       & $180$ at $j=191$    & $57$ at $j=48$ \\
726	$|P|$            & $1963$              & $698$ \\
727	Largest prime     & $181$ digits       & $68$ digits \\
728	\hline
729	\end{tabular}
730	\caption{Summary of the two runs of \texttt{sunconj.py}}\label{tab:summary}
731	\end{table}
732	
733	\subsection{Proof of Theorem~\ref{thm:main_plus}: $r = 2$ via $1/(p+1)$}\label{sec:proof_plus}
734	
735	Run the program \texttt{sunconj.py} on the target $r = 2$ with the
736	command
737	\begin{verbatim}
738	  python sunconj.py +1 2 false true
739	\end{verbatim}
740	From Example~\ref{ex:phase1},
741	Phase~1 produces the multiset
742	\begin{equation}\label{eq:M0_plus}
743	  M_0^{+} \;=\; \{\,2,2,3,3,5,5,7,11,13,17,19,19,23,43,3467,4005539\,\},
744	\end{equation}
745	of size $16$, with $\ct(M_0^{+}) = 4$ (four excess copies, namely the
746	second copies of $2$, $3$, $5$, and $19$). 
747	
748	Phase~2 then runs for $452$ iterations. The trajectory of $\ct$ is
749	plotted in Figure~\ref{fig:traj_plus}. 
750	It is roughly unimodal: $\ct$ rises
751	(with occasional downward fluctuations) from $4$ at iteration $0$
752	to a peak of $180$ at iteration $191$, then decreases
753	(with occasional upward fluctuations) to $0$ at iteration $451$.
754	
755	\begin{figure}[h]

780	\end{axis}
781	\end{tikzpicture}
782	\caption{Trajectory of $\ct$ during Phase~2 for $(r,s) = (2,+1)$}
783	\label{fig:traj_plus}
784	\end{figure}
785	
786	
787	After $452$ iterations the program outputs a line
788	\texttt{RESGOOD~$2$~[2,3,5,\ldots]~1963}, 
789	which lists the elements of
790	the set
791	\[
792	  P_+ \;=\; \{p_1, p_2, \ldots, p_{1963}\} \;\subset\; \PP
793	\]
794	in increasing order. The full list is contained in the supplementary
795	file 
796	\texttt{prime\_set\_P\_\allowbreak plus2.txt}. 
797	We verify three properties:
798	\begin{enumerate}
799	\item \emph{All elements are prime.} 
800	
801	\item \emph{All elements are distinct.} The output list, sorted in
802	  increasing order, has length $1963$. 
803	
804	\item \emph{The sum equals $2$ exactly.} The sum
805	  $\sum_{p \in P_+} 1/(p+1)=2$ is computed in arbitrary-precision rational
806	  arithmetic.
807	\end{enumerate}
808	The smallest element of $P_+$ is $2$, the largest is a prime with $181$
809	decimal digits. This proves Theorem~\ref{thm:main_plus}. \hfill$\Box$
810	
811	\subsection{Proof of Theorem~\ref{thm:main_minus}: $r = 3$ via $1/(p-1)$}\label{sec:proof_minus}
812	
813	Run the program with the command
814	\begin{verbatim}
815	  python sunconj.py -1 3 false true
816	\end{verbatim}
817	($s=-1$, target $r=3$). Phase~1 produces the initial multiset
818	\begin{equation}\label{eq:M0_minus}
819	  M_0^{-} \;=\; \{\,2, 3, 3, 5, 5, 7, 11, 13, 17, 19, 37, 241\,\},
820	\end{equation}
821	of size $12$, with $\ct(M_0^{-}) = 2$ (two excess copies, namely the
822	second copies of $3$ and $5$). One checks directly in exact rational
823	arithmetic that
824	\[
825	  S^{(-1)}(M_0^{-})
826	  \;=\; \frac{1}{1} + \frac{2}{2}  + \frac{2}{4} 
827	        + \frac{1}{6} + \frac{1}{10} + \frac{1}{12} + \frac{1}{16}
828	        + \frac{1}{18} + \frac{1}{36} + \frac{1}{240}
829	  \;=\; 3.
830	\]
831	Phase~2 runs for only $167$ iterations (compared to $452$ for the
832	$s=+1$ case), peaking at $\ct = 57$ at $j = 48$ and then decreasing to
833	$0$ at $j=166$. The trajectory is plotted in Figure~\ref{fig:traj_minus}.
834	
835	\begin{figure}[h]
836	\centering
837	\begin{tikzpicture}
838	\begin{axis}[
839	  width=0.95\textwidth,
840	  height=7cm,
841	  xlabel={iteration $j$},
842	  ylabel={excess count $\ct$},
843	  xmin=0, xmax=170,
844	  ymin=0, ymax=65,
845	  xtick={0,20,40,60,80,100,120,140,160},
846	  ytick={0,10,20,30,40,50,60},
847	  grid=both,
848	  grid style={very thin, gray!20},
849	  major grid style={thin, gray!30},
850	  tick label style={font=\small},
851	  label style={font=\small},
852	  legend style={font=\small, at={(0.97,0.97)}, anchor=north east, draw=none},
853	]
854	\addplot[thick, teal!70!black, mark=none]
855	  coordinates {(0,2) (1,4) (2,9) (3,13) (4,14) (5,15) (6,16) (7,18) (8,19) (9,19) (10,19) (11,22) (12,25) (13,26) (14,27) (15,27) (16,27) (17,29) (18,30) (19,32) (20,36) (21,36) (22,37) (23,41) (24,40) (25,40) (26,41) (27,43) (28,46) (29,44) (30,45) (31,45) (32,46) (33,47) (34,47) (35,46) (36,47) (37,48) (38,48) (39,48) (40,48) (41,50) (42,50) (43,49) (44,50) (45,50) (46,52) (47,54) (48,57) (49,57) (50,57) (51,55) (52,55) (53,55) (54,55) (55,56) (56,55) (57,55) (58,55) (59,55) (60,54) (61,54) (62,54) (63,55) (64,55) (65,55) (66,55) (67,55) (68,56) (69,56) (70,55) (71,56) (72,55) (73,55) (74,55) (75,53) (76,51) (77,51) (78,52) (79,52) (80,51) (81,50) (82,50) (83,50) (84,50) (85,49) (86,50) (87,51) (88,51) (89,50) (90,49) (91,49) (92,49) (93,49) (94,49) (95,48) (96,46) (97,46) (98,45) (99,45) (100,46) (101,45) (102,45) (103,45) (104,45) (105,42) (106,39) (107,39) (108,39) (109,39) (110,38) (111,38) (112,38) (113,37) (114,36) (115,36) (116,36) (117,35) (118,35) (119,35) (120,35) (121,36) (122,35) (123,34) (124,33) (125,32) (126,32) (127,31) (128,30) (129,29) (130,26) (131,26) (132,26) (133,26) (134,26) (135,26) (136,25) (137,22) (138,22) (139,22) (140,20) (141,20) (142,20) (143,20) (144,20) (145,18) (146,18) (147,18) (148,18) (149,16) (150,16) (151,15) (152,15) (153,13) (154,12) (155,11) (156,10) (157,9) (158,9) (159,8) (160,7) (161,5) (162,4) (163,3) (164,2) (165,1) (166,0)};
856	\addlegendentry{$\ct(M_j)$}
857	\addplot[only marks, mark=*, mark size=2pt, color=red!80!black]
858	  coordinates {(48,57)};
859	\addlegendentry{peak ($j{=}48$, $\ct{=}57$)}
860	\end{axis}
861	\end{tikzpicture}
862	\caption{Trajectory of $\ct$ during Phase~2 for  $(r,s) = (3,-1)$}
863		\label{fig:traj_minus}
864	\end{figure}
865	
866	The qualitative shape of the trajectory is the same as in
867	Figure~\ref{fig:traj_plus}: a climb, a noisy plateau, and a monotone
868	descent.
869	
870	After $167$ iterations the program outputs a line
871	\texttt{RESGOOD~$3$~[2,3,5,\ldots]~698}, which lists the elements of
872	the set
873	\[
874	  P_- \;=\; \{q_1, q_2, \ldots, q_{698}\} \;\subset\; \PP
875	\]
876	in increasing order. The full list is contained in the supplementary
877	file 
878	\texttt{prime\_set\_P\_\allowbreak minus3.txt}. 
879	The same three verifications
880	as in Section~\ref{sec:proof_plus} are performed:
881	\begin{enumerate}
882	\item all $698$ elements are prime;
883	\item the list, sorted in increasing order, has length $698$;
884	\item the sum $\sum_{p \in P_-} 1/(p-1)=3$
885	  exactly.
886	\end{enumerate}
887	The smallest element of $P_-$ is $2$, and the largest is a prime with $68$ decimal digits. This proves
888	Theorem~\ref{thm:main_minus}. \hfill$\Box$
889	
890	\subsection{Remarks on the two sets}
891	
892	\begin{remark}
893	The sets $P_+$ and $P_-$ produced by our algorithm are not claimed to
894	be optimal in any sense. Their cardinalities depend on the splitting
895	tables and on the choice of beam-search candidates; smaller sets
896	certainly exist. Finding the minimum $|P|$ such that
897	$\sum_{p \in P} 1/(p+s) = r$ is a natural open question for each
898	given pair $(r, s)$.
899	\end{remark}
900	
901	\begin{remark}
902	The largest primes in $P_+$ and $P_-$ have $181$ and $68$ decimal
903	digits respectively. Such large primes are not surprising: the
904	identity~\eqref{eq:split} roughly squares the denominator at each
905	splitting step, and several chained splittings during Phase~2 can
906	compound this growth.
907	\end{remark}
908

Since you're using the web interface and `sunconj.py` is small (~24 KB), just upload it through the browser:

1. Go to your repo page on **github.com**.
2. Click **Add file** (top right) → **Upload files**.
3. Drag `sunconj.py` from your file manager into the upload area — or click **choose your files** and pick `/home/guoniu/cl/sunconj/public_html/sunconj.py`.
4. Scroll down, type a commit message (e.g. "Add sunconj.py"), click **Commit changes**.

That's it — the file appears in the repo, and your README's link `[sunconj.py](sunconj.py)` will now work.

**Tip:** you can upload **many files at once** in that same step — drag `sunconj.py`, `sunconj_frac.py`, `sunconj_prime.py`, `sunconj_power.py`, the `data_*.txt`, `prime_set_*.txt`, `sunconj.pdf`, and the `.gz` files all together, then commit once. That's faster than doing them one at a time, and it makes all the README links resolve in a single go.

One reminder: the two big `output_frac_*_1000.txt.gz` files must each be **under 25 MB** for the web uploader to accept them. Everything else here is well under the limit.

=== ASSISTANT  (2026-07-07T16:01:13.978Z) ===
Same as before — GitHub replaces a file when you upload one with the **same name and same path**. Two web ways:

## For a single small text file (e.g. edited `README.md` or `sunconj.py`)
Editing in the browser is quickest:
1. On the repo, click the file to open it.
2. Click the **pencil icon** (✏️ Edit).
3. Make your changes (or paste the new content).
4. Scroll down → **Commit changes**.

## For files you edited locally (any number)
Re-upload and overwrite:
1. **Add file** → **Upload files**.
2. Drag in the modified file(s) — **keep the same filename**.
3. Because the name matches, GitHub overwrites the old version.
4. Type a commit message → **Commit changes**.

Each commit is saved as a new version, so nothing is lost — you can always see or restore earlier versions from the file's **History**.

**Two things to watch:**
- **Filename must match exactly.** `sunconj.py` overwrites `sunconj.py`. If you rename it (e.g. `sunconj_v2.py`), GitHub treats it as a *new* file and the old one stays — you'd then have both.
- **Same folder.** If a file lives in a subfolder, upload it into that same subfolder, or you'll create a duplicate at the top level.

If you find yourself re-uploading often, the command-line `git` workflow (`git add`, `git commit`, `git push`) is much faster for repeated updates — but for occasional edits, the web overwrite is perfectly fine.

=== ASSISTANT  (2026-07-07T16:18:31.837Z) ===
Confirmed against **all 304,191 lines** — both proposed bounds hold with **zero violations**. Here's what the data says.

## Result

Writing each line as `a/b`, with `p` = largest prime and `l` = number of primes:

| bound | formula | violations / 304,191 | binding case |
|---|---|---|---|
| **length** | `l ≤ fl(a,b) = b + 7` | 0 | `47/52` → l = 59 = 52+7 |
| **max prime** | `p ≤ fp(a,b) = 10^b` | 0 | `1/… ` small-b lines (ratio ≈ 0.78) |

So the two simplest valid envelopes are:

$$\boxed{\,f_l(a,b) = b + 7\,}\qquad\boxed{\,f_p(a,b) = 10^{\,b}\,}$$

## What this means (nice restatements)

- **`l ≤ b + 7`** — the decomposition of `a/b` never uses more than `b + 7` primes. (Tight: equality is actually reached at `47/52`.)
- **`p < 10^b`** ⇔ **the largest prime has at most `b` decimal digits.** The empirical envelope is only `log₁₀ p ≤ 0.78·b`, so `10^b` is a safe, clean over-estimate.

## Notes on the choice

- **Both bounds depend only on `b`, not `a`.** I tested this carefully: over the full list, `l` and `p` are governed essentially by the denominator `b`; the numerator `a` has negligible effect on the maxima. So the *simplest* valid functions genuinely use `b` alone. (If you specifically want an `a`-dependent form, I can fit one, but it won't be simpler or tighter.)
- **Tighter simple alternatives**, if you prefer less slack:
  - length: `l ≤ 2b` (max ratio `l/b` is 1.23, at `17/26`) — but `b+7` is far tighter for large `b`.
  - max prime: `p ≤ 10^{0.8b}` (also 0 violations) — but `10^b` is the round, memorable version.
- `p ≤ b^b` also holds but is enormously loose (and even has 1 violation at the tiny `b` end), so it's not a good choice.

Want me to run the **same analysis on `data_frac_minus_1000.txt`** (the `s = -1` case) to check whether `b+7` and `10^b` survive there too — or is the `+1` file enough?

=== ASSISTANT  (2026-07-07T16:29:45.684Z) ===
No worries — you've got it right. It's a plain **real-number inequality**: `|P| ≤ log₂ q + 1`, with `|P|` an integer on the left and a real number on the right. No floor or ceil is needed or intended.

For example, at `q = 76369` (the longest `+1` case, `l = 13`): `log₂(76369) + 1 ≈ 16.22 + 1 = 17.22`, and indeed `13 ≤ 17.22`. ✓

If you ever *did* want an integer-valued right-hand side, the natural tightening would be `|P| ≤ ⌊log₂ q⌋ + 1`, which also holds here (`⌊16.22⌋ + 1 = 17`). But it's not necessary — the real-valued form is cleaner and correct as written, so I'll leave the table as is.

=== ASSISTANT  (2026-07-07T16:42:13.689Z) ===
`sunconj_reciprocal.py` is written and working for both shifts. It's the copy-and-modify you asked for:

**What changed from `sunconj_prime.py`:**
- Loops over **all** integers `d = 2..M` (not just primes).
- **Captures and parses** `sunconj.py`'s `RESGOOD 1/d [...] l` line (via `subprocess` instead of `os.system`).
- **Checks the conjecture** per `d`: `|P| ≤ log₂d + 1` and `max P ≤ d^18` (constants `LEN_CONST=1`, `PRIME_EXP=18` at the top, easy to tweak).
- Prints a **summary**: failures, length-bound violations, prime-bound violations, largest observed exponent `log_d(max P)`, tightest length slack, and a final verdict.

**Usage:**
```
python sunconj_reciprocal.py M [SHIFT] [VERBOSE] [prefer_large]
```

For your full run (`d` up to 10000), both shifts, quietly, saving output:
```
python sunconj_reciprocal.py 10000 +1 false true > recip_plus_10000.txt
python sunconj_reciprocal.py 10000 -1 false true > recip_minus_10000.txt
```

**On the range — a heads-up (the `(?)` in your question):** this runs `sunconj.py` once per `d`, so ~10,000 subprocess launches per shift. Given the prime run to `10⁵` was already flagged as "very long," `d` to 10⁴ over *all* integers will still take a good while (Python startup dominates for small `d`, real computation for large `d`). Two practical notes:
- Start with `false` verbose so you only see violations + the final summary (quieter, marginally faster).
- If it's too slow, drop to `M=2000` first as a sanity pass, then scale up.

- **A dense floor near exponent 1–2** — most `d` (the ones where the greedy step lands on a small prime) have a tiny largest prime. The band right at `≈1` is all the `d` whose decomposition's biggest prime is `~d` itself.
- **A noisy cloud that widens as `d` grows** — the *typical* worst case drifts up from ~5 to ~10–13 across the range, and the **spread keeps increasing**. There's no visible flat ceiling.
- **Rare spikes** poke far above the cloud: the `+1` outlier at **`d=869` → 18.97** (the lone `d^18` violation) and the `−1` peak at **`d=1399` → 16.34**.

**Implication for the conjecture — the `d^18` exponent is fragile:**

The single spike already pierces `d^18`, and because the envelope is *growing and noisy* rather than flat, committing to any fixed integer exponent looks risky — a bigger spike could appear at larger `d`. Two honest ways to state it:

1. **Bump + hedge:** `max P ≤ d^{19}` for `d ≤ 2000` (largest observed 18.97), but note it's empirical and not clearly asymptotic.
2. **Reframe (safer):** drop the hard number — *"`max P ≤ d^{C}` for some absolute constant `C`; empirically `C ≈ 19` suffices for `d ≤ 2000`."* — or even question whether a fixed `C` exists at all, given the upward drift.

The **length bound `|P| ≤ log₂d + 1` remains clean and unviolated** — that half of the conjecture is solid; it's only the max-prime exponent that's shaky.

A couple of options for next steps, your call:
- **Extend the range** (e.g. `d ≤ 5000` or the full `10000`) to see whether spikes keep approaching/crossing ~19 — this decides between "bump to 19" and "reframe with `C`".
- **Try `prefer_large=false`** — the peaks may be an artifact of the large-prime-preferring mode; that mode gave much smaller exponents in the old `d ≤ 1000` fraction data. If you want, I can plot both modes together to see which the conjecture should be stated for.

Want me to extend the range, or compare the two `prefer_large` modes on the same plot?

Yes — I see it clearly now, and the data is striking:

**The two modes are identical at 1997 of 1998 denominators.** The *only* place they differ is `d=869`:

|---|---|---|
| `prefer_large=True` | **18.97** | same as False |
| `prefer_large=False` | **3.37** | same as True |

- Denominators where False is worse than True: **0**.

So `prefer_large=False` **weakly dominates** — it matches `True` at every `d` except the one pathological spike, which it flattens from 18.97 down to 3.37. There's no `d` where you pay for it.

**The consequence you spotted:** with `prefer_large=False`, the worst exponent over `d ≤ 2000` drops to **14.87** (at `d=1657`) — comfortably **under 18**. In other words, the lone `d^18` violation was a `True`-mode artifact at `d=869`; in `False` mode the `d^18` bound *survives* over the whole range. Good call.

**But one honest caveat** — your earlier instinct still holds: even in `False` mode the envelope keeps climbing (14.87, 14.49, 14.00 … all at the *largest* `d`: 1657, 1823, 1142 …). So `False` removes the crazy outlier and buys headroom, but the exponent is still slowly growing with `d`. A constant exponent may still break eventually — just much later than in `True` mode.

Two clean paths from here:
1. **Adopt `prefer_large=False` as the canonical mode** and re-verify — likely `d^18` (or `d^{15}`) holds well past 2000. Simple, but empirically capped.

| | envelope behavior | conjecture health |
|---|---|---|

| `|P|` (length) | stays flat-ish while `log₂d+1` rises → **gap grows** | robust — bound gets *safer* with `d` |

149	The same algorithm, run on a wide range of inputs, also establishes
150	the following further verifications.
151	
152	\begin{theorem}[Further verified cases]\label{thm:families}
153	For each shift $s \in \{+1, -1\}$, Theorems~\ref{conj:sun_plus}
154	and~\ref{conj:sun_minus} hold for every rational $r$ in the following
155	families:
156	\begin{enumerate}
157	\item all reduced fractions $a/b$ with $1 \leq a < b \leq 1000$;
158	\item all reciprocals $1/q$ for primes $q \leq 100\,000$;
159	\item all reciprocals $1/2^k$ for integers $1 \leq k \leq 40$.
160	\end{enumerate}
161	In each case an explicit set $P$ of distinct primes with
162	$\sum_{p \in P} 1/(p+s) = r$ is produced and verified in exact rational
163	arithmetic. Moreover, the produced sets are of controlled size: for every
164	$r$ in each family, the largest prime $\max P$ and the number of primes
165	$|P|$ satisfy the bounds collected in Table~\ref{tab:bounds}. In particular,
166	for the fraction family~(1) with $s = +1$, writing $b$ for the reduced
167	denominator of $r$,
168	\begin{equation}\label{eq:frac_bounds_plus}
169	  \max P \;\leq\; 10^{\,b}, \qquad\qquad |P| \;\leq\; b + 7 .
170	\end{equation}
171	\end{theorem}
172	
173	\begin{table}[h]
174	\centering
175	\begin{tabular}{l c c c}
176	\hline
177	Family & shift $s$ & bound on $\max P$ & bound on $|P|$ \\
178	\hline
179	Fractions $a/b$, $1\le a<b\le 1000$   & $+1$ & $10^{\,b}$    & $b+7$ \\
180	                                      & $-1$ & $10^{\,b}$    & $b+8$ \\
181	Prime reciprocals $1/q$, $q\le 10^5$  & $+1$ & $q^{18}$      & $\log_2 q + 1$ \\
182	                                      & $-1$ & $q^{18}$      & $\log_2 q + 1$ \\
183	Power reciprocals $1/2^k$, $k\le 40$  & $+1$ & $2^{16k}$     & $k+1$ \\
184	                                      & $-1$ & $2^{16k}$     & $k+1$ \\
185	\hline
186	\end{tabular}
187	\caption{Size bounds for the produced sets $P$: the largest prime $\max P$
188	and the cardinality $|P|$, as functions of the relevant parameter of each
189	family ($b$ = reduced denominator for family~(1); $q$ for family~(2);
190	$k$ for family~(3)).}
191	\label{tab:bounds}
192	\end{table}
193	
194	\begin{remark}
195	The bounds in Table~\ref{tab:bounds} are established by direct verification
196	over the (finite) families; they are properties of the particular sets $P$
197	produced by \texttt{sunconj.py}, not of every possible decomposition. For
198	family~(1) with $s=+1$ the bounds~\eqref{eq:frac_bounds_plus} were checked
199	over all $304{,}191$ reduced fractions $a/b$ with $b\le 1000$. The length
200	bound is sharp: equality $|P| = b+7$ occurs, for instance, at $r = 47/52$.
201	The bound $\max P \le 10^{\,b}$ says equivalently that the largest prime has
202	at most $b$ decimal digits; the observed envelope is in fact
203	$\max P \le 10^{\,0.8\,b}$, and $10^{\,b}$ is retained only for simplicity.
204	For $s=-1$ the same digit bound $\max P \le 10^{\,b}$ holds---now nearly
205	attained, the observed envelope being $\max P \le 10^{\,0.99\,b}$---while the
206	length bound weakens by one to $|P| \le b+8$, attained at $r = 35/46$.
207	
208	For the prime family~(2) the bounds take a different form, reflecting that
209	$1/q$ is a single unit fraction: the decompositions are short, with
210	$|P| \le \log_2 q + 1$ (the maximal length observed is only $13$, for both
211	shifts and all $9592$ primes $q\le 10^5$), while the largest prime is
212	bounded by the fixed power $\max P \le q^{18}$ (the exponent $18$ is the
213	smallest integer that works; $q^{17}$ already fails). The power family~(3)
214	behaves in the same way, since $1/2^k$ is again a single unit fraction:
215	$|P| \le k+1$ (the observed maximum is only $10$, at $k=40$) and
216	$\max P \le 2^{16k}$. Indeed, writing $D$ for the denominator of $r$,
217	families~(2) and~(3) both obey the uniform bounds
218	\[
219	  |P| \;\le\; \log_2 D + 1, \qquad\qquad \max P \;\le\; D^{18},
220	\]
221	reflecting that a single reciprocal $1/D$ requires only logarithmically many
222	terms, in contrast to the general fractions of family~(1).
223	\end{remark}
224	
225	The length bound $|P| \le \log_2 D + 1$ is common to families~(2)
226	and~(3) --- two rather different kinds of denominator (a prime and a power
227	of $2$) obeying the very same logarithmic bound. This suggests that it
228	holds for the reciprocal of an arbitrary integer.
229	
230	\begin{conjecture}\label{conj:reciprocal}
231	Let $d \ge 2$ be an integer and let $s \in \{+1, -1\}$. Then $1/d$ admits a
232	prime decomposition
233	\[
234	  \sum_{p \in P} \frac{1}{p+s} \;=\; \frac{1}{d}
235	  \qquad (P \subset \PP \text{ a finite set of distinct primes})
236	\]
237	whose length satisfies
238	\[
239	  |P| \;\le\; \log_2 d + 1 .
240	\]
241	\end{conjecture}
242	
243	We have verified Conjecture~\ref{conj:reciprocal} for both shifts and for
244	every integer $2 \le d \le 3000$, as well as for all primes $d \le 10^5$ and
245	all powers $d = 2^k$ with $k \le 40$ (Table~\ref{tab:bounds}). The bound is
246	attained only at $d = 2$, where $|P| = 2 = \log_2 2 + 1$, and gains slack as
247	$d$ grows: the longest decomposition found for $d \le 3000$ has $|P| = 10$,
248	against $\log_2 3000 + 1 \approx 12.55$. Whether the logarithmic length bound
249	is optimal remains open.
250	
251	We deliberately do not conjecture a companion bound on the largest prime
252	$\max P$. Empirically its exponent $\log_d(\max P)$ is erratic and grows with
253	$d$, so no fixed power of $d$ appears to bound it for all reciprocals --- in
254	sharp contrast to the tame behaviour of $\max P$ within the finite families
255	of Table~\ref{tab:bounds}.
256	
257	The proofs are constructive and use exactly the same algorithm, run

**Done — `max P` is now gone from Theorem 5.** Summary of what changed:

| Element | Before | After |
|---|---|---|
| Theorem statement | bounded both `max P` and `|P|` (eq. `10^b`/`b+7`) | only `|P| ≤ b+7` |
| Table `tab:bounds` | 4 cols incl. `max P` (`10^b`, `q^18`, `2^{16k}`) | 3 cols, **`|P|` only** |
| Remark | discussed digit bounds, `q^18`, uniform `D^18` | length story only (`b+7`/`b+8`, `log₂q+1`, `k+1`, uniform `log₂D+1`) |

Based on the search results, I found the paper you're looking for:

**"Unit fractions with shifted prime denominators"** by Thomas F. Bloom, published on arXiv (identifier 2305.02689) on May 4, 2023.

**Main results:**

The paper proves that any positive rational number is the sum of distinct unit fractions with denominators in {p-1 : p prime}. The same conclusion holds for the set {p-h : p prime} for any h∈ℤ\{0}, provided a necessary congruence condition is satisfied. The paper also proves that this is true for any subset of the primes of relative positive density, provided a necessary congruence condition is satisfied.

The paper was later published in the Proceedings of the Royal Society of Edinburgh Section A: Mathematics in 2024.

=== ASSISTANT  (2026-07-07T17:20:57.762Z) ===
I've now read the core of Bloom's paper (arXiv 2305.02689, = ref [1]). Here's the verdict.

## No — Conjecture 7 does not follow from [1].

Bloom's paper gives you the **existence** half of the statement but nothing remotely like the **length bound**, and its method structurally cannot produce it. Three concrete reasons:

**1. Bloom's result is purely qualitative / non-effective.**
He states this explicitly (p. 3):
> *"To minimise technicalities we have proved only a qualitative form of Theorem 1. In principle a (very weak) quantitative version could be proved with the same methods … but this would complicate the presentation significantly."*

So [1] provides *no* explicit bound at all — not on term count, not on denominators. Even the "very weak" quantitative version he alludes to would be governed by `log log N` / density quantities, i.e. astronomically far from `log₂ d`.

**2. The construction inherently uses a huge number of terms — the opposite of few.**
The engine is Proposition 1/2 + Corollary 2: take a set `A ⊂ [1,N]` (N "sufficiently large", infinitely many N) whose reciprocal mass `∑1/n ≳ δ log log N` is large, then extract a subset `S` with `∑_{n∈S} 1/n = 1/d`. Representing `1/d` by carving an exact subset out of a set carrying `~log log N` of reciprocal mass yields **very many** terms, with primes running up to an unbounded `N`. Corollary 2 even iterates Theorem 1 `r` times for `x=r/q`. Nothing in the argument controls — or could yield — a term count, and certainly not `≤ log₂ d + 1`.

**3. Denominators are unbounded in `d`.**
The primes live in `[1,N]` for `N` "sufficiently large depending on…", so [1] gives no bound on the largest prime as a function of `d` either. (This is consistent with what we found empirically for `max P` — its exponent grows — but Bloom doesn't even bound *that*.)

## What [1] *does* give you

Only Theorems 1–2 of your paper (the bare existence: some finite `P` with `∑1/(p+s)=1/d` exists). Your **Conjecture 7 is strictly stronger and quantitative**: it asserts a *short* representation, `|P| ≤ log₂ d + 1`. That logarithmic count is essentially the doubling efficiency of your splitting/beam algorithm — a constructive phenomenon Bloom's density method neither addresses nor implies.

**Bottom line for the paper:** Conjecture 7 is genuinely open and *not* a corollary of [1]. If anything, you can safely add a sentence noting that Bloom [1] settles existence but non-constructively and with no control on the number of terms, so the logarithmic length bound of Conjecture 7 lies beyond its reach. Want me to add such a remark after Conjecture 7?

Sources:
- [Bloom, arXiv:2305.02689](https://arxiv.org/abs/2305.02689)

205	and~(3) --- two rather different kinds of denominator (a prime and a power
206	of $2$) obeying the very same logarithmic bound. This suggests that it
207	holds for the reciprocal of an arbitrary integer.
208	
209	\begin{conjecture}\label{conj:reciprocal}
210	Let $d \ge 2$ be an integer and let $s \in \{+1, -1\}$. Then $1/d$ admits a
211	prime decomposition
212	\[
213	  \sum_{p \in P} \frac{1}{p+s} \;=\; \frac{1}{d}
214	  \qquad (P \subset \PP \text{ a finite set of distinct primes})
215	\]
216	whose length satisfies
217	\[
218	  |P| \;\le\; \log_2 d + 1 .
219	\]
220	\end{conjecture}
221	
222	We have verified Conjecture~\ref{conj:reciprocal} for both shifts and for
223	every integer $2 \le d \le 3000$, as well as for all primes $d \le 10^5$ and
224	all powers $d = 2^k$ with $k \le 40$ (Table~\ref{tab:bounds}). The bound is
225	attained only at $d = 2$, where $|P| = 2 = \log_2 2 + 1$, and gains slack as
226	$d$ grows: the longest decomposition found for $d \le 3000$ has $|P| = 10$,
227	against $\log_2 3000 + 1 \approx 12.55$. Whether the logarithmic length bound
228	is optimal remains open.
229	
230	We deliberately do not conjecture a companion bound on the largest prime
231	$\max P$: empirically its exponent $\log_d(\max P)$ is erratic and grows with
232	$d$, so no fixed power of $d$ appears to bound it for all reciprocals.
233	
234	The proofs are constructive and use exactly the same algorithm, run
235	with different inputs: the sets $P_+$ and $P_-$ are produced by the
236	program \texttt{sunconj.py} described in Section~\ref{sec:algo} and
237	listed in full in the supplementary files \texttt{prime\_set\_P\_plus2.txt}
238	and \texttt{prime\_set\_P\_minus3.txt}. All identities are verified
239	in arbitrary-precision rational arithmetic (\texttt{gmpy2.mpq}).
240	
241	\smallskip
242

244	The constructions and the program described in this paper were

Clean two-pass compile, 0 undefined references. The note is now in, immediately after Conjecture 7:

> The existence of *some* such decomposition is already guaranteed by Bloom's Theorems 1 and 2; the content of Conjecture 7 lies entirely in the *length* bound. Bloom's proof, however, settles existence non-constructively and gives no control on the number of terms: it extracts an exact sub-sum equal to `1/d` from a set of shifted primes of large reciprocal mass, so the representations it yields are long and involve primes of unbounded size. The logarithmic bound `|P| ≤ log₂d + 1` is therefore beyond the reach of [1], and appears to be a genuinely constructive, algorithmic phenomenon.

This makes the relationship precise for the reader: [1] supplies existence, but the quantitative logarithmic-length statement is new and not derivable from it — cross-referenced to your Theorems 1–2 and `\cite{Bloom}`.

=== ASSISTANT  (2026-07-07T17:27:55.652Z) ===
Honestly — no, I don't think it earns a place in the paper.

**Why it's weak here:** the length plot is mostly a flat cloud of small integers (1–10) sitting under a smooth log curve, with a large empty gap above it. That's exactly the *point* of the conjecture (`|P|` stays well below `log₂d+1`, gap widens), but it's a "nothing goes wrong" picture — visually it's a lot of white space and not much structure. Everything it conveys is already stated more precisely in three lines of text + Table 1:
- max `|P| = 10` for `d ≤ 3000`,
- curve reaches `≈12.55`,
- tight only at `d = 2`, slack grows after.

A reviewer is likely to read such a figure as padding rather than insight.

**Contrast:** the figure that *was* genuinely informative — the exponent plot, with the drift and the `d=869` spike — belonged to the `max P` story you deliberately cut. The interesting visual went with the part you removed; what's left (length) is the well-behaved part that a sentence handles better than a scatter.

**The paper already has its earning-their-keep figures** — the two `ct`-trajectory plots in the proof section, which show real non-monotone structure. Those justify being figures; the length scatter doesn't clear the same bar.

**My recommendation:** leave the length graph out of the paper. Keep `recip_length.png` in the GitHub repo as supplementary evidence (a reader who wants to see the data can find it there), and let Table 1 + the conjecture text carry it in the paper.

If you *do* want one visual for the conjecture, the only version I'd consider is a compact plot of just the **running-max envelope** of `|P|` against `log₂d+1` (drops the noisy cloud, shows only the boundary) — but even that is marginal, and I'd still lean toward omitting it. Want me to try that stripped-down version so you can judge, or drop the figure idea entirely?
