[tool result]
1	"""
2	sunconj.py — Prime Unit-Fraction Decomposition (Sun's Conjecture)
3	==================================================================
4	
5	Goal
6	----
7	Given a positive rational number r, express r as a finite sum of distinct
8	unit fractions of the form 1/(p + SHIFT), where p ranges over prime numbers
9	and SHIFT ∈ {+1, −1} is a fixed global parameter:
10	
11	    r = 1/(p₁ + SHIFT) + 1/(p₂ + SHIFT) + … + 1/(pₖ + SHIFT),
12	    with p₁ < p₂ < … < pₖ  (all prime).
13	
14	This is related to a conjecture by Zhi-Wei Sun (2015) asserting that every
15	positive rational has such a representation.  The default SHIFT = +1 targets
16	fractions 1/(p+1); set SHIFT = -1 to use 1/(p−1).
17	
18	Algorithm overview
19	------------------
20	1. A *greedy* pass picks the largest feasible prime p at each step (largest
21	   unit fraction that does not exceed the remainder).
22	2. A *structured beam search* (testL / expand_candidates) explores several
23	   candidate primes at each depth level, then falls back to greedy for the
24	   tail.
25	3. A *splitting* loop (reduce_duplicates / split_prime) iteratively replaces
26	   any repeated prime p appearing m times with a fresh decomposition of
27	   m·(1/(p+SHIFT)), removing multiplicity until the representation uses
28	   every prime at most once.
29	
30	Usage
31	-----
32	    python sunconj.py           # verifies all k/n with gcd(k,n)=1, n ≤ 200
33	    python sunconj.py 100       # same, up to n = 100
34	
35	    from sunconj import decompose
36	    print(decompose(mpq(3, 7)))  # → sorted list of primes
37	
38	Requirements
39	------------
40	    pip install gmpy2
41	"""
42	
43	import sys
44	import gmpy2
45	from gmpy2 import mpz, mpq, is_prime
46	from functools import lru_cache
47	
48	# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
49	# Global configuration
50	# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
51	
52	sys.setrecursionlimit(10_000)
53	
54	# SHIFT determines which unit fractions are used:
55	#   SHIFT = +1  →  1/(p+1)   (default, Sun's original conjecture)
56	#   SHIFT = -1  →  1/(p-1)
57	SHIFT: int = +1
58	
59	# When True, print verbose progress information during the search.
60	VERBOSE: bool = False
61	
62	# Cache: maps (prime p, multiplicity m) → list of primes that represent
63	#   m * 1/(p + SHIFT)  as a sum of distinct unit fractions 1/(q + SHIFT).
64	_split_cache: dict = {}
65	
66	
67	# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
68	# Utility helpers
69	# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
70	
71	def unit_fraction(p) -> mpq:
72	    """Return the exact rational 1 / (p + SHIFT)."""
73	    return mpq(1, int(p) + SHIFT)
74	
75	
76	def prime_sum(primes: list) -> mpq:
77	    """
78	    Return the exact rational sum  Σ 1/(p + SHIFT)  for p in *primes*.
79	
80	    Uses gmpy2.mpq for arbitrary-precision arithmetic; no floating-point
81	    rounding errors.
82	    """
83	    total = mpq(0)
84	    for p in primes:
85	        total += unit_fraction(p)
86	    return total
87	
88	
89	def multiplicity_dict(primes: list) -> dict:
90	    """
91	    Return a dict  {prime: count}  from a list of primes (possibly repeated).
92	
93	    Example:  [2, 3, 3, 5]  →  {2: 1, 3: 2, 5: 1}
94	    """
95	    counts: dict = {}
96	    for p in primes:
97	        counts[p] = counts.get(p, 0) + 1
98	    return counts
99	
100	
101	def count_duplicates(primes: list) -> int:
102	    """
103	    Return the total number of *excess* occurrences (i.e. total repetitions
104	    minus the number of distinct primes that repeat ≥ 2 times).
105	
106	    A return value of 0 means every prime appears exactly once.
107	
108	    Example:  [2, 3, 3, 5, 5, 5]  →  (1 extra 3) + (2 extra 5s) = 3
109	    """
110	    excess = 0
111	    for cnt in multiplicity_dict(primes).values():
112	        if cnt >= 2:
113	            excess += cnt - 1
114	    return excess
115	
116	
117	# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
118	# Fraction tables  (ways to write 1 as a sum of 2 or 3 unit fractions)
119	# These are used to *split* a single term r into 2 or 3 sub-problems whose
120	# sum equals r, potentially making each sub-problem easier to solve.
121	# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
122	
123	def _reduced_fractions_up_to(bound: int) -> list:
124	    """
125	    Return all reduced fractions j/k with 1 ≤ j < k < bound and gcd(j,k)=1,
126	    as mpq objects, stored in a set for O(1) membership testing.
127	    """
128	    fracs = set()
129	    for k in range(2, bound):
130	        for j in range(1, k):
131	            if gmpy2.gcd(j, k) == 1:
132	                fracs.add(mpq(j, k))
133	    return fracs
134	
135	
136	def _build_one_as_two(bound: int) -> list:
137	    """
138	    Return all ordered pairs [a, b] of reduced fractions with a < b = 1−a,
139	    both having denominator < bound.  Each pair satisfies a + b = 1.
140	    """
141	    fracs = _reduced_fractions_up_to(bound)
142	    pairs = []
143	    for a in fracs:
144	        if a < mpq(1, 2) and (1 - a) in fracs:
145	            pairs.append([a, 1 - a])
146	    return pairs
147	
148	
149	def _build_one_as_three(bound: int) -> list:
150	    """
151	    Return all ordered triples [a, b, c] of reduced fractions with
152	    a < b < c and a + b + c = 1, all having denominator < bound.
153	    """
154	    fracs = _reduced_fractions_up_to(bound)
155	    frac_list = sorted(f for f in fracs if f < mpq(1, 2))
156	    triples = []
157	    for a in frac_list:
158	        if a >= mpq(1, 3):
159	            break
160	        for b in frac_list:
161	            if b <= a:
162	                continue
163	            c = 1 - a - b
164	            if c in fracs and c > b:
165	                triples.append([a, b, c])
166	    return triples
167	
168	
169	# Pre-build the split tables at three depth levels.
170	# "Fast"   uses small tables → quick but may miss some decompositions.
171	# "Middle" uses medium tables.
172	# "Deep"   uses large tables → slow but more thorough.
173	
174	_SPLIT_TABLES: dict = {}
175	
176	def _init_split_tables():
177	    global _SPLIT_TABLES
178	    _SPLIT_TABLES["Fast"]   = [[mpq(1, 1)]] + _build_one_as_two(10)  + _build_one_as_three(7)
179	    _SPLIT_TABLES["Middle"] = [[mpq(1, 1)]] + _build_one_as_two(20)  + _build_one_as_three(13)
180	    _SPLIT_TABLES["Deep"]   = [[mpq(1, 1)]] + _build_one_as_two(30)  + _build_one_as_three(17)
181	
182	_init_split_tables()
183	
184	
185	# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
186	# Core greedy prime search
187	# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
188	
189	def smallest_feasible_prime(remainder: mpq, min_prime) -> mpz | None:
190	    """
191	    Find the *smallest* prime p ≥ min_prime such that
192	
193	        1/(p + SHIFT) ≤ remainder,
194	
195	    i.e. p is the natural next greedy step.
196	
197	    Returns None if remainder ≤ 0.
198	    """
199	    if remainder <= 0:
200	        return None
201	
202	    # Lower bound on p: from 1/(p+SHIFT) ≤ r we get p ≥ 1/r − SHIFT.
203	    # Subtract 2 for safety against rounding, then also enforce p > min_prime.
204	    p_lb = max(int(1 / remainder) - 2, 1)
205	    p_lb = max(p_lb, int(min_prime) + 1)
206	    p = mpz(p_lb)
207	
208	    while True:
209	        if is_prime(p) and remainder >= unit_fraction(p):
210	            return p
211	        p += 1
212	
213	
214	def greedy_decompose(remainder: mpq, min_prime=1, cutoff=10**8, max_steps=99) -> list | bool:
215	    """
216	    Greedy algorithm: repeatedly subtract the largest feasible unit fraction
217	    1/(p + SHIFT) until remainder reaches 0.
218	
219	    Parameters
220	    ----------
221	    remainder : mpq
222	        The rational number to decompose.
223	    min_prime : int
224	        All selected primes must be strictly greater than this value.
225	    cutoff : int
226	        If the selected prime exceeds *cutoff*, restart the tail search with
227	        a squared cutoff (allows very large primes).
228	    max_steps : int
229	        Maximum number of primes to add before giving up.
230	
231	    Returns
232	    -------
233	    list of int  on success,  False  on failure.
234	    """
235	    current = remainder
236	    last_prime = min_prime
237	    primes_found = []
238	
239	    for _ in range(max_steps):
240	        p = smallest_feasible_prime(current, last_prime)
241	        if p is None:
242	            return False
243	        primes_found.append(int(p))
244	        current -= unit_fraction(p)
245	        last_prime = p
246	
247	        if current == 0:
248	            return primes_found
249	
250	        if p > cutoff:
251	            # Try again with a much larger allowed cutoff for remaining tail.
252	            if cutoff < 10**30:
253	                return False
254	            tail = greedy_decompose(current, min_prime=int(last_prime),
255	                                    cutoff=cutoff**2, max_steps=max_steps)
256	            if tail is False:
257	                return False
258	            return primes_found[:-1] + [primes_found[-1]] + tail
259	
260	    return False
261	
262	
263	# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
264	# Beam / candidate expansion
265	# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
266	
267	def _num_candidates(mode: str) -> int:
268	    """Number of candidate primes to generate per level in beam search."""
269	    return {"Fast": 3, "Middle": 4, "Deep": 6}[mode]
270	
271	
272	def generate_prime_candidates(remainder: mpq, min_prime, mode: str = "Fast") -> list:
273	    """
274	    Generate a short list of candidate (primes_so_far, new_remainder) pairs
275	    by stepping through consecutive primes above *min_prime*.
276	
277	    Each candidate is  [[p], remainder − 1/(p+SHIFT)].
278	
279	    The number of candidates depends on *mode*:
280	      Fast   → 3 candidates
281	      Middle → 4 candidates
282	      Deep   → 6 candidates
283	    """
284	    n = _num_candidates(mode)
285	    candidates = []
286	    prev_p = min_prime
287	    for _ in range(n):
288	        p = smallest_feasible_prime(remainder, prev_p)
289	        if p is None:
290	            break
291	        candidates.append([[p], remainder - unit_fraction(p)])
292	        prev_p = p
293	    return candidates
294	
295	
296	def beam_search_decompose(remainder: mpq, min_prime=1, max_steps=99,
297	                          mode: str = "Fast") -> list | bool:
298	    """
299	    Structured beam search for a prime decomposition of *remainder*.
300	
301	    Expands *generate_prime_candidates* for several levels (CT iterations),
302	    then calls *greedy_decompose* on the surviving frontier nodes.
303	
304	    Returns the solution with minimum prime-sum (heuristic for simplicity),
305	    or False if none found.
306	    """
307	    # Number of beam-expansion rounds before falling back to greedy.
308	    expansion_rounds = 1 if mode == "Fast" else 3
309	
310	    # Frontier: list of  [primes_chosen_so_far, remaining_value]
311	    frontier = generate_prime_candidates(remainder, min_prime, mode)
312	    solutions = []
313	
314	    # Expand the beam for several rounds.
315	    for _ in range(expansion_rounds):
316	        next_frontier = []
317	        for node_primes, node_rem in frontier:
318	            if node_rem == 0:
319	                solutions.append(node_primes)
320	                continue
321	            last_p = node_primes[-1]
322	            children = generate_prime_candidates(node_rem, last_p, mode)
323	            for child_primes, child_rem in children:
324	                merged = sorted(child_primes + node_primes)
325	                next_frontier.append([merged, child_rem])
326	        frontier = next_frontier
327	
328	    # For each surviving node, complete with greedy.
329	    for node_primes, node_rem in frontier:
330	        if node_rem == 0:
331	            solutions.append(node_primes)
332	            continue
333	        tail = greedy_decompose(node_rem, min_prime=node_primes[-1])
334	        if tail is not False:
335	            solutions.append(node_primes + tail)
336	
337	    if not solutions:
338	        return False
339	
340	    # Return the solution with smallest sum of primes (fewest / smallest primes).
341	    return min(solutions, key=sum)
342	
343	
344	# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
345	# Split-table based decomposition  (check1 / check1list / check)
346	# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
347	
348	def _decompose_via_split_table(target: mpq, min_prime: int,
349	                               mode: str) -> list | bool:
350	    """
351	    Try to decompose *target* by first writing it as
352	
353	        target = w₁·target + w₂·target + …   (where [w₁, w₂, …] ∈ split table)
354	
355	    and solving each piece independently with beam_search_decompose.
356	
357	    Returns a flat sorted list of primes on success, or:
358	      ["PARTIAL", todo_list, solved_primes]  if exactly one piece failed.
359	    False if completely stuck.
360	    """
361	    table = _SPLIT_TABLES[mode]
362	    best_partial = None
363	
364	    for weights in table:
365	        # Each weight vector sums to 1; multiply through by target.
366	        sub_targets = [w * target for w in weights]
367	        results = [beam_search_decompose(st, min_prime, mode=mode)
368	                   for st in sub_targets]
369	
370	        failed = results.count(False)
371	
372	        if failed == 0:
373	            # Full solution found: concatenate all sub-results.
374	            combined = []
375	            for res in results:
376	                combined.extend(res)
377	            return combined
378	
379	        if failed == 1 and best_partial is None:
380	            # Remember the best partial solution (one piece unsolved).
381	            todo = [sub_targets[i] for i, r in enumerate(results) if r is False]
382	            solved = [p for res in results if res for p in res]
383	            best_partial = ["PARTIAL", todo, solved]
384	
385	    if best_partial and best_partial[2]:
386	        return best_partial
387	    return False
388	
389	
390	def _decompose_list(targets: list, min_prime: int, mode: str) -> list | bool:
391	    """
392	    Decompose each value in *targets* independently and combine results.
393	
394	    If all succeed → return flat solved prime list.
395	    If exactly some fail → return ["PARTIAL", unsolved_targets, solved_primes].
396	    If any is completely stuck → return False.
397	    """
398	    todo: list = []
399	    solved: list = []
400	
401	    for t in targets:
402	        result = _decompose_via_split_table(t, min_prime, mode)
403	        if result is False:
404	            return False
405	        if isinstance(result, list) and result[0] == "PARTIAL":
406	            todo.extend(result[1])
407	            solved.extend(result[2])
408	        else:
409	            solved.extend(result)
410	
411	    return ["PARTIAL", todo, solved] if todo else solved
412	
413	
414	def decompose_rational(target: mpq, min_prime: int,
415	                       mode: str = "Fast") -> list | bool:
416	    """
417	    Main recursive decomposition driver.
418	
419	    Iterates up to 5 rounds of *_decompose_list*, carrying over unsolved
420	    sub-targets from one round to the next.  Returns a sorted list of
421	    primes whose unit fractions sum to *target*, or False on failure.
422	    """
423	    pending = [target]
424	    all_solved: list = []
425	
426	    for _ in range(5):
427	        result = _decompose_list(pending, min_prime, mode)
428	        if result is False:
429	            return False
430	        if not (isinstance(result, list) and result[0] == "PARTIAL"):
431	            all_solved.extend(result)
432	            return sorted(all_solved)
433	        # Carry forward unsolved pieces.
434	        pending = result[1]
435	        all_solved.extend(result[2])
436	
437	    return False
438	
439	
440	# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
441	# Prime splitting  (replace  m·(1/(p+SHIFT))  by distinct primes)
442	# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
443	
444	def _split_prime_multiplicity(prime: int, multiplicity: int,
445	                              mode: str = "Fast") -> list | bool:
446	    """
447	    Find a list of distinct primes  q₁, q₂, …  such that
448	
449	        multiplicity · 1/(prime + SHIFT)
450	            = 1/(q₁ + SHIFT) + 1/(q₂ + SHIFT) + …
451	
452	    where none of the qᵢ equal *prime* (so the multiplicity is eliminated).
453	
454	    Tries nu = multiplicity, multiplicity−1, … 1 copies of 1/(prime+SHIFT)
455	    in the residual, storing the best split in _split_cache.
456	
457	    Returns sorted list of primes (including the kept copies of *prime*) or False.
458	    """
459	    global _split_cache
460	
461	    for kept in range(multiplicity, 0, -1):
462	        # Residual after keeping *kept* copies of prime:
463	        #   (multiplicity − kept) · 1/(prime + SHIFT)  needs a fresh split.
464	        residual = mpq(kept, int(prime) + SHIFT)
465	        pass  # (set DEBUG=True in source to trace inner residuals)
466	
467	        result = decompose_rational(residual, prime, mode=mode)
468	        if result is not False:
469	            _split_cache[(prime, kept)] = result
470	            extra_copies = [prime] * (multiplicity - kept)
471	            return sorted(result + extra_copies)
472	
473	    return False
474	
475	
476	def _lookup_split_cache(prime: int, multiplicity: int) -> list | bool:
477	    """
478	    Return a cached split for (prime, multiplicity) if available,
479	    otherwise False.
480	
481	    The cache stores the replacement for *kept* copies of the prime's unit
482	    fraction, where kept ≤ multiplicity.  Any remaining (multiplicity − kept)
483	    copies of prime are appended directly.
484	    """
485	    if (prime, multiplicity) in _split_cache:
486	        cached = _split_cache[(prime, multiplicity)]
487	        # multiplicity copies were requested; cache entry covers all of them.
488	        return sorted(cached)
489	    return False
490	
491	
492	def expand_repeated_prime(prime: int, multiplicity: int,
493	                          mode: str = "Fast") -> list | bool:
494	    """
495	    Replace *multiplicity* copies of *prime* in a decomposition by a list of
496	    distinct primes, using the cache when possible.
497	
498	    This is the public interface for splitting.
499	    """
500	    # Try cache first.
501	    cached = _lookup_split_cache(prime, multiplicity)
502	    if cached is not False:
503	        return cached
504	    # Compute and cache.  Only print if verbose, to avoid cluttering the
505	    # main progress line.
506	    if VERBOSE:
507	        print(f"    Computing split: DO[({prime},{multiplicity})]", flush=True)
508	    result = _split_prime_multiplicity(prime, multiplicity, mode=mode)
509	    return result
510	
511	
512	# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
513	# Deduplication loop  (make all primes distinct)
514	# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
515	
516	def reduce_duplicates(primes: list, mode: str = "Fast") -> list | bool:
517	    """
518	    Given a list *primes* (possibly with repetitions), replace the first
519	    repeated prime p (with multiplicity m) by an equivalent set of *distinct*
520	    primes using *expand_repeated_prime*.
521	
522	    Returns:
523	      - The updated sorted list (possibly still with other duplicates), or
524	      - The same list unchanged if no duplicates remain, or
525	      - False if the split for the repeated prime failed.
526	    """
527	    L = sorted(primes)
528	
529	    # Find the first prime that appears more than once.
530	    repeated = None
531	    for i in range(len(L) - 1):
532	        if L[i] == L[i + 1]:
533	            repeated = L[i]
534	            break
535	
536	    if repeated is None:
537	        return L  # Already duplicate-free.
538	
539	    count = L.count(repeated)
540	    # Remove all but one copy (we will replace count−1 via split).
541	    for _ in range(count - 1):
542	        L.remove(repeated)
543	
544	    replacement = expand_repeated_prime(repeated, count - 1, mode=mode)
545	    if replacement is False:
546	        return False
547	
548	    L.extend(replacement)
549	    return sorted(L)
550	
551	
552	# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
553	# Top-level decomposition with full deduplication
554	# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
555	
556	def decompose(target: mpq, initial_primes: list | None = None,
557	              prefer_large: bool = False) -> list | bool:
558	    """
559	    Decompose *target* into distinct primes  p₁ < p₂ < … < pₖ  such that
560	
561	        target = Σ 1/(pᵢ + SHIFT).
562	
563	    Parameters
564	    ----------
565	    target : mpq
566	        The positive rational to decompose.
567	    initial_primes : list or None
568	        Skip the initial search and start deduplication from this list.
569	    prefer_large : bool
570	        If True, use "Middle" mode for splitting (finds slightly larger but
571	        sometimes cleaner representations).
572	
573	    Returns
574	    -------
575	    Sorted list of distinct primes, or False if no decomposition found.
576	    """
577	    if initial_primes is None:
578	        # Try modes in increasing order of thoroughness.
579	        primes = decompose_rational(target, 1, mode="Fast")
580	        if primes is False:
581	            primes = decompose_rational(target, 1, mode="Middle")
582	        if primes is False:
583	            primes = decompose_rational(target, 1, mode="Deep")
584	        if primes is False:
585	            return False
586	    else:
587	        primes = list(initial_primes)
588	
589	    split_mode = "Middle" if prefer_large else "Fast"
590	
591	    # Iteratively eliminate all duplicate primes.
592	    #
593	    # Progress is always printed so you can watch ct converge to 0:
594	    #
595	    #   loop j=<iter>  ct=<dups>  trend=<arrow>  [targeting prime=<p>]
596	    #
597	    #   ct     = number of excess prime copies still to eliminate
598	    #   trend  = down (good: converging), up (temporarily worse), flat (no change)
599	    #
600	    # With VERBOSE=True, each line also shows which prime is being targeted,
601	    # and the final solution list is printed.
602	    #
603	    prev_dups = count_duplicates(primes)
604	    _TREND = {-1: "down", 0: "flat", 1: "up"}
605	
606	    for iteration in range(10_000):
607	        dups = count_duplicates(primes)
608	        trend = _TREND[0 if dups == prev_dups else (-1 if dups < prev_dups else 1)]
609	
610	        # Build the progress line.
611	        line = f"{target}, loop j={iteration:>5}  ct={dups:>5}  trend={trend}"
612	        if VERBOSE:
613	            counts = multiplicity_dict(primes)
614	            repeated_now = next((p for p in sorted(counts) if counts[p] >= 2), None)
615	            if repeated_now:
616	                line += f"  targeting prime={repeated_now}"
617	        print(line, flush=True)
618	
619	        if dups == 0:
620	            break
621	
622	        prev_dups = dups
623	
624	        updated = reduce_duplicates(primes, mode=split_mode)
625	        if updated is False:
626	            updated = reduce_duplicates(primes, mode="Middle")
627	        if updated is False:
628	            updated = reduce_duplicates(primes, mode="Deep")
629	        if updated is False:
630	            break
631	
632	        primes = updated
633	
634	    final_dups = count_duplicates(primes)
635	    if final_dups > 0:
636	        print(f"  FAILED: stuck at ct={final_dups}.")
637	        return False
638	
639	    if VERBOSE:
640	        print(f"  SUCCESS: {len(primes)} distinct primes: {primes}")
641	
642	    sol= sorted(list(set(primes)))
643	    if sol is not False:
644	        print(f"RESGOOD {target} {[int(p) for p in sol]} {len(sol)}")
645	    else:
646	        print(f"RESBAD  {r} FALSE")
647	
648	    return sol 
649

652	# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
653	# Entry point
654	# ---------------------------------------------------------------------------
655	
656	def parse_number_string(s: str):
657	    if '/' in s:
658	        num_str, denom_str = s.split('/', 1)
659	        p = int(num_str)
660	        q = int(denom_str)
661	        if q == 0:
662	            raise ValueError("Denominator cannot be zero")
663	        if q < 0:
664	            p = -p
665	            q = -q
666	        return p, q
667	    else:
668	        return int(s), 1
669	
670	if __name__ == "__main__":
671	    if len(sys.argv) < 3:
672	        print("Usage: python sunconj.py SHIFT string_of_number [VERBOSE] [prefer_large]")
673	        print("Example: python sunconj.py +1 2 ")
674	        print("Example: python sunconj.py -1 3 ")
675	        print("Example: python sunconj.py +1 7/4 ")
676	        sys.exit(1)
677	
678	    shift_str = sys.argv[1]
679	    num_str = sys.argv[2]
680	
681	    # Parse SHIFT
682	    if shift_str == '+1':
683	        SHIFT = 1
684	    elif shift_str == '-1':
685	        SHIFT = -1
686	    else:
687	        print("Error: SHIFT must be +1 or -1")
688	        sys.exit(1)
689	
690	    # Parse VERBOSE (default False)
691	    VERBOSE = False 
692	    if len(sys.argv) > 3:
693	        verbose_str = sys.argv[3].lower()
694	        if verbose_str in ('true', '1', 'yes'):
695	            VERBOSE = True
696	        elif verbose_str in ('false', '0', 'no'):
697	            VERBOSE = False
698	        else:
699	            print("Error: VERBOSE must be True/False, 1/0, or yes/no")
700	            sys.exit(1)
701	
702	    # Parse prefer_large (default True)
703	    prefer_large = True
704	    if len(sys.argv) > 4:
705	        prefer_large_str = sys.argv[4].lower()
706	        if prefer_large_str in ('true', '1', 'yes'):
707	            prefer_large = True
708	        elif prefer_large_str in ('false', '0', 'no'):
709	            prefer_large = False
710	        else:
711	            print("Error: prefer_large must be True/False, 1/0, or yes/no")
712	            sys.exit(1)
713	
714	    # Parse the number string
715	    try:
716	        p, q = parse_number_string(num_str)
717	        number = mpq(p, q)
718	    except ValueError as e:
719	        print(f"Error: {e}")
720	        sys.exit(1)
721	
722	    print(f"Decomposing {number} with SHIFT = {SHIFT:+d}, prefer_large = {prefer_large}, VERBOSE = {VERBOSE}")
723	
724	    decompose(number, prefer_large=prefer_large)
725

It only needs `gmpy2` now (no dependency on `sunconj.py` at runtime), so it's suitable to ship in the repo as the reproducibility script for Conjecture 7.

1	# A constructive approach to unit fractions with shifted prime denominators
2	
3	**Guo-Niu Han** — 2026/05/02
4	
5	## Abstract
6	
7	A conjecture of Zhi-Wei Sun states that every positive rational number
8	can be written as a finite sum of unit fractions whose denominators are
9	of the form *p*+1 or *p*−1, where *p* runs over distinct primes.
10	Both cases have recently been proved in full generality by Thomas Bloom.
11	However, even for small integers such as *r*=2 or *r*=3,
12	finding an explicit decomposition is highly non-trivial: the required
13	primes can have hundreds of decimal digits, and the construction
14	involves hundreds of elimination steps.
15	We exhibit explicit decompositions for these two integer cases:
16	the case *r*=2 with denominators *p*+1, using a set
17	of 1963 distinct primes, and the case *r*=3 with denominators
18	*p*−1, using a set of 698 distinct primes.
19	The algorithm behind these constructions — based on beam search
20	and splitting tables — may be of independent interest for related
21	problems.

56	
57	\begin{abstract}
58	A conjecture of Zhi-Wei Sun states that every positive rational number
59	can be written as a finite sum of unit fractions whose denominators are
60	of the form $p+1$ or $p-1$, where $p$ runs over distinct primes.
61	Both cases have recently been proved in full generality by
62	Thomas Bloom~\cite{Bloom}. Bloom's theorems are existence results;
63	even for small integers such as $r=2$ or $r=3$, producing an explicit
64	decomposition remains highly non-trivial, as the required primes can
65	have hundreds of decimal digits and the construction involves hundreds
66	of elimination steps.
67	We give a computational construction of explicit, certified prime sets
68	for two integer cases --- $r=2$ with denominators $p+1$, using a set of
69	$1963$ distinct primes, and $r=3$ with denominators $p-1$, using a set
70	of $698$ distinct primes --- and describe a beam-search method, guided
71	by splitting tables, that also succeeds on several finite families of
72	rational inputs. All identities are verified in exact rational
73	arithmetic, and the programs and data are made available in a public
74	repository.
75	\end{abstract}
76	
77	
78	
79	% -------------------------------------------------------------------
80	\section{Introduction}
81	% -------------------------------------------------------------------
82	
83	The representation of a positive rational as a sum of distinct unit fractions
84	$1/n$ is a classical topic going back to Egyptian fraction expansions~\cite{ES,Graham}.
85	A more recent variant restricts the denominators to lie in arithmetic
86	sets of the form $\{p+s : p \text{ prime}\}$ for a fixed integer shift $s$.
87	Two natural choices are $s = +1$ and $s = -1$, which lead to the
88	following results, initially conjectured by Sun~\cite{Sun} and recently
89	proved by Thomas Bloom~\cite{Bloom}.
90	
91	\begin{theorem}[Bloom~\cite{Bloom}]\label{conj:sun_plus}
92	For every positive rational number $r$, there exists a finite set $P$
93	of prime numbers such that
94	\[
95	  r \;=\; \sum_{p \in P} \frac{1}{p+1}.
96	\]
97	\end{theorem}
98	
99	\begin{theorem}[Bloom~\cite{Bloom}]\label{conj:sun_minus}
100	For every positive rational number $r$, there exists a finite set $P$
101	of prime numbers such that
102	\[
103	  r \;=\; \sum_{p \in P} \frac{1}{p-1}.
104	\]
105	\end{theorem}
106	
107	Sun had verified these statements for many small rationals.
108	Qing-Hu Hou has verified them for all rational
109	numbers $r \in (0,1)$ with denominator not exceeding $100$~\cite{Sun}.
110	However, even for small integers such as $r=2$ or $r=3$,
111	finding an explicit decomposition is highly non-trivial: the required
112	primes can have hundreds of decimal digits, and the construction
113	involves hundreds of elimination steps.
114	
115	Since Bloom's theorems settle existence but do not yield explicit
116	decompositions, the emphasis of the present paper is different: our
117	contribution is to provide explicit, independently certified examples
118	together with a practical computational method. Concretely, we give
119	such explicit constructions for the two integer cases $r=2$ (shift
120	$s=+1$) and $r=3$ (shift $s=-1$), by means of a beam-search algorithm
121	that may also serve as a starting point for related problems.
122	
123	\begin{theorem}[Case $r = 2$, $s = +1$]\label{thm:main_plus}
124	There exists a set $P_+$ of $1963$ distinct prime numbers, the largest of
125	which has $181$ decimal digits and equals
126	\begin{center}
127	{\begin{minipage}{0.75\textwidth}
128	\normalfont\small\seqsplit{2361944081811262661581420029993779369004749258112890856900708493649883531810055173157404200152116544006497944823707101618004503106964633107234444169742408558510043346214786314674399}
129	\end{minipage}}
130	\end{center}
131	such that
132	\[
133	  \sum_{p \in P_+} \frac{1}{p+1} \;=\; 2.
134	\]
135	\end{theorem}
136	
137	\begin{theorem}[Case $r = 3$, $s = -1$]\label{thm:main_minus}
138	There exists a set $P_-$ of $698$ distinct prime numbers, the largest of
139	which has $68$ decimal digits and equals
140	\[
141	21405811313494409045983653494328316787629317190709732873740497324001,
142	\]
143	such that
144	\[
145	  \sum_{p \in P_-} \frac{1}{p-1} \;=\; 3.
146	\]
147	\end{theorem}
148	
149	The same algorithm, run on a wide range of inputs, also establishes
150	the following further verifications.
151	
152	\begin{theorem}[Further verified cases]\label{thm:families}
153	For each shift $s \in \{+1, -1\}$, Theorems~\ref{conj:sun_plus}
154	and~\ref{conj:sun_minus} hold for every rational $r$ in the following
155	families:
156	\begin{enumerate}
157	\item all reduced fractions $a/b$ with $1 \leq a < b \leq 1000$;
158	\item all reciprocals $1/q$ for primes $q \leq 100\,000$;
159	\item all reciprocals $1/2^k$ for integers $1 \leq k \leq 40$.
160	\end{enumerate}
161	In each case an explicit set $P$ of distinct primes with
162	$\sum_{p \in P} 1/(p+s) = r$ is produced and verified in exact rational
163	arithmetic. Moreover, the produced sets are short: for every $r$ in each
164	family, the number of primes $|P|$ satisfies the bound collected in
165	Table~\ref{tab:bounds}. In particular, for the fraction family~(1), writing
166	$b$ for the reduced denominator of $r$, one has $|P| \le b + 8$ for both
167	shifts.
168	\end{theorem}
169	
170	\begin{table}[h]
171	\centering
172	\begin{tabular}{l c c}
173	\hline
174	Family & shift $s$ & bound on $|P|$ \\
175	\hline
176	Fractions $a/b$, $1\le a<b\le 1000$   & $\pm 1$ & $b+8$ \\
177	Prime reciprocals $1/q$, $q\le 10^5$  & $\pm 1$ & $\log_2 q + 1$ \\
178	Power reciprocals $1/2^k$, $k\le 40$  & $\pm 1$ & $k+1$ \\
179	\hline
180	\end{tabular}
181	\vspace{2ex}
182	\caption{Length bounds for the produced sets $P$}
183	\label{tab:bounds}
184	\end{table}
185	
186	\begin{remark}
187	The bounds in Table~\ref{tab:bounds} are established by direct verification
188	over the (finite) families; they are properties of the particular sets $P$
189	produced by \texttt{sunconj.py}, not of every possible decomposition. For
190	family~(1) the bound $|P| \le b+8$ was checked, for both shifts, over all
191	$304{,}191$ reduced fractions $a/b$ with $b\le 1000$. It is sharp for $s=-1$,
192	where equality $|P| = b+8$ occurs at $r = 35/46$; for $s=+1$ the length is in
193	fact one smaller, $|P| \le b+7$ (attained at $r = 47/52$).
194	
195	For the reciprocal families~(2) and~(3) the decompositions are much shorter,
196	reflecting that $1/q$ and $1/2^k$ are single unit fractions:
197	$|P| \le \log_2 q + 1$ for all $9592$ primes $q\le 10^5$ (the maximal length
198	observed is only $13$), and $|P| \le k+1$ for the powers (maximum $10$, at
199	$k=40$). Writing $D$ for the denominator of $r$, both families obey the
200	uniform bound $|P| \le \log_2 D + 1$, reflecting that a single reciprocal
201	$1/D$ requires only logarithmically many terms, in contrast to the general
202	fractions of family~(1).
203	\end{remark}
204	
205	The length bound $|P| \le \log_2 D + 1$ is common to families~(2)
206	and~(3) --- two rather different kinds of denominator (a prime and a power
207	of $2$) obeying the very same logarithmic bound. This suggests that it
208	holds for the reciprocal of an arbitrary integer.
209	
210	\begin{conjecture}\label{conj:reciprocal}
211	Let $d \ge 2$ be an integer and let $s \in \{+1, -1\}$. Then $1/d$ admits a
212	prime decomposition
213	\[
214	  \sum_{p \in P} \frac{1}{p+s} \;=\; \frac{1}{d}
215	  \qquad (P \subset \PP \text{ a finite set of distinct primes})
216	\]
217	whose length satisfies
218	\[
219	  |P| \;\le\; \log_2 d + 1 .
220	\]
221	\end{conjecture}
222	
223	The existence of \emph{some} such decomposition is already guaranteed by
224	Bloom's Theorems~\ref{conj:sun_plus} and~\ref{conj:sun_minus}; the content of
225	Conjecture~\ref{conj:reciprocal} lies entirely in the \emph{length} bound.
226	Bloom's proof, however, settles existence non-constructively and gives no
227	control on the number of terms: it extracts an exact sub-sum equal to $1/d$
228	from a set of shifted primes of large reciprocal mass, so the representations
229	it yields are long and involve primes of unbounded size. The logarithmic
230	bound $|P| \le \log_2 d + 1$ is therefore beyond the reach of~\cite{Bloom},
231	and appears to be a genuinely constructive, algorithmic phenomenon.
232	
233	We have verified Conjecture~\ref{conj:reciprocal} for both shifts and for
234	every integer $2 \le d \le 3000$, as well as for all primes $d \le 10^5$ and
235	all powers $d = 2^k$ with $k \le 40$ (Table~\ref{tab:bounds}). The bound is
236	attained only at $d = 2$, where $|P| = 2 = \log_2 2 + 1$, and gains slack as
237	$d$ grows: the longest decomposition found for $d \le 3000$ has $|P| = 10$,
238	against $\log_2 3000 + 1 \approx 12.55$. Whether the logarithmic length bound
239	is optimal remains open.
240	
241	We deliberately do not conjecture a companion bound on the largest prime
242	$\max P$: empirically its exponent $\log_d(\max P)$ is erratic and grows with
243	$d$, so no fixed power of $d$ appears to bound it for all reciprocals.
244	
245	\medskip
246	
247	The proofs are constructive and use exactly the same algorithm, run
248	with different inputs: the sets $P_+$ and $P_-$ are produced by the
249	program \texttt{sunconj.py} described in Section~\ref{sec:algo} and
250	listed in full in the supplementary files \texttt{prime\_set\_P\_plus2.txt}
251	and \texttt{prime\_set\_P\_minus3.txt}. All identities are verified
252	in arbitrary-precision rational arithmetic (\texttt{gmpy2.mpq}).
253	
254	\smallskip
255	
256	{\it Historical remark.}
257	The constructions and the program described in this paper were
258	completed in 2018. The paper was not submitted
259	at that time for personal reasons. It is presented here for the
260	explicit prime sets and the algorithmic contribution.
261	
262	
263	All programs and data files are available in a permanent public
264	repository, allowing independent verification of the computations:
265	\begin{center}
266	\url{https://github.com/GuoniuHan/sunconj}
267	\end{center}
268	
269	The paper is organised as follows.
270	Section~\ref{sec:notation} introduces notation.
271	Section~\ref{sec:algo} describes the algorithm, with worked examples.
272	Section~\ref{sec:example} illustrates the first three iterations on
273	the run $(r=2,s=+1)$.
274	Section~\ref{sec:proof} gives the proofs of
275	Theorems~\ref{thm:main_plus} and~\ref{thm:main_minus}.
276	
277	
278	% -------------------------------------------------------------------
279	\section{Notation and setting}\label{sec:notation}
280	% -------------------------------------------------------------------
281	
282	We denote by $\PP$ the set of primes. Throughout the paper, $s$ denotes
283	a fixed shift in $\{+1, -1\}$. For a finite multiset $M$ of primes
284	we write
285	\[
286	  S(M) \;=\; \sum_{p \in M} \frac{1}{p+s}
287	\]
288	(counting multiplicities). When the shift needs to be displayed
289	explicitly we write $S^{(s)}(M)$. A \emph{prime decomposition} of a
290	positive rational $r$ at shift $s$ is a finite \emph{set}
291	$P \subset \PP$  such that
292	$S^{(s)}(P) = r$. 
293	
294	A central elementary identity for our algorithm is
295	\begin{equation}\label{eq:split}
296	  \frac{1}{p+s} \;=\; \frac{1}{p+s+1} \;+\; \frac{1}{(p+s)(p+s+1)},
297	\end{equation}
298	which splits one term $1/(p+s)$ into two terms with strictly larger
299	denominators. More generally, for a prime $p$ and a positive integer $m$,
300	one is led to the following sub-problem:
301	
302	\begin{quote}
303	\textit{Find a finite set $Q$ of primes with $\min Q > p$ such that}
304	\[
305	  \frac{m}{p+s} \;=\; \sum_{q \in Q} \frac{1}{q+s}.
306	\]
307	\end{quote}
308	
309	We call such a $Q$ a \emph{prime replacement} for the triple $(p,m,s)$. The
310	existence of prime replacements for every $(p,m)$ would imply
311	Theorems~\ref{conj:sun_plus} and~\ref{conj:sun_minus} constructively for all
312	positive rationals. In our algorithm we do not solve this sub-problem in full
313	generality. Instead, we solve it heuristically for the finitely many triples
314	$(p,m,s)$ that arise during a run, and we verify each replacement in exact
315	arithmetic.
316	\medskip
317	
318	A crucial concept of our approach is the \emph{excess count} of a
319	multiset. For a finite multiset $M$ of primes we define
320	\[
321	  \ct(M) \;=\; \sum_{p \in M, \, \mu_M(p) \geq 2} \bigl(\mu_M(p) - 1\bigr),
322	\]
323	where $\mu_M(p)$ is the multiplicity of $p$ in $M$. Thus $\ct(M)=0$
324	if and only if $M$ is a set. The whole point of Phase~2 of the algorithm
325	is to drive $\ct$ down to $0$.
326	
327	
328	% -------------------------------------------------------------------
329	\section{The algorithm}\label{sec:algo}
330	% -------------------------------------------------------------------
331	
332	The algorithm is implemented in the Python program \texttt{sunconj.py},
333	which uses \texttt{gmpy2}~\cite{gmpy2} for exact rational arithmetic and primality
334	testing. The shift $s \in \{+1, -1\}$ is a parameter; on the cases
335	treated here, the program runs in roughly $6$ minutes on a standard
336	desktop computer for $(r,s) = (2,+1)$, and in well under a minute for
337	$(r,s) = (3,-1)$.
338	
339	\subsection{Outline}
340	
341	The algorithm has two phases.
342	\begin{description}
343	\item[Phase~1.] \emph{Initial multiset.}
344	  Find a multiset $M_0$ of primes with $S^{(s)}(M_0) = r$.
345	\item[Phase~2.] \emph{Deduplication.}
346	  Iteratively replace one repeated prime at a time, until the multiset
347	  becomes a set.
348	\end{description}
349	Both phases share a common subroutine, the \emph{beam search} of
350	Section~\ref{sec:beam}, which produces decompositions of a rational
351	$r$ as a sum of distinct unit fractions $1/(p+s)$ with $p$ prime
352	and $p$ above a prescribed lower bound.
353	
354	\subsection{Splitting tables}\label{sec:tables}
355	
356	The beam search is guided by a precomputed list of partitions of $1$
357	into two or three reduced fractions; we call this list a
358	\emph{splitting table}. Concretely, we use:
359	\begin{itemize}
360	\item all pairs $(a, 1-a)$ with $a$ a reduced fraction $j/k$,
361	  $1 \leq j < k$, denominator $k$ below a small bound;
362	\item all triples $(a, b, 1-a-b)$, ordered $a < b < 1-a-b$, with
363	  the same denominator constraint;
364	\item the trivial table entry $[1]$.
365	\end{itemize}
366	Three tables are built, of increasing size and thoroughness:
367	\textsf{Fast} (denominators $<10$ for pairs, $<7$ for triples),
368	\textsf{Middle} ($<20$ and $<13$),
369	\textsf{Deep} ($<30$ and $<17$).
370	
371	Given a target rational $r$ and a table entry $[w_1,\ldots,w_t]$
372	(with $w_1+\cdots+w_t=1$), the algorithm replaces the single problem
373	``decompose $r$'' by the $t$ independent sub-problems
374	``decompose $w_i r$'' ($i=1,\ldots,t$), each handled by a separate
375	call to the beam search, since
376	$$w_1 r+\cdots+w_t r=r.$$
377	%
378	Since $w_i <1$, the sub-problems are ``easier'' because each
379	$w_i r$ is strictly smaller than $r$, so the greedy search reaches
380	zero in fewer steps.
381	
382	This trick is essential: a direct greedy expansion of a generic
383	rational $r$ tends to produce extremely long sequences of primes,
384	or to fail to terminate within reasonable cutoffs,
385	whereas after splitting via a table entry the individual
386	sub-rationals are typically solvable in a few steps.
387	
388	
389	The \textsf{Fast} table contains $29$ entries: the trivial entry $[1]$,
390	the $27$ ordered pairs $(j/k,\, 1-j/k)$ with $\gcd(j,k)=1$,
391	$1 \leq j < k < 10$, and the single triple with denominator below~$7$:
392	\[
393	  \frac{1}{6} + \frac{1}{3} + \frac{1}{2} \;=\; 1.
394	\]
395	The first few pairs, in the order they appear in the table, are
396	\[
397	  \frac{1}{2}+\frac{1}{2},\quad
398	  \frac{1}{3}+\frac{2}{3},\quad
399	  \frac{1}{4}+\frac{3}{4},\quad
400	  \frac{1}{5}+\frac{4}{5},\quad
401	  \frac{2}{5}+\frac{3}{5},\quad
402	  \frac{1}{6}+\frac{5}{6},\quad\ldots
403	\]
404	
405	
406	See Examples~\ref{ex:split} and \ref{ex:phase1} for illustrations of how the splitting tables work.
407	
408	\subsection{Beam search}\label{sec:beam}
409	
410	The beam search is the core decomposer. Given a rational $r > 0$ and
411	a lower bound $b$, it seeks a list of distinct primes
412	$b< p_1 < p_2 < \cdots < p_k$ 
413	with 
414	$\sum 1/(p_i+s) = r$. It proceeds as follows.
415	
416	\begin{enumerate}
417	\item \emph{Candidate generation.} For each rational
418	  remainder encountered, list the $n$ smallest primes
419	  $p > b$ with $1/(p+s) \leq r$. The width $n$ is $3$, $4$, or $6$
420	  in modes \textsf{Fast}, \textsf{Middle}, \textsf{Deep} respectively.
421	
422	
423	\item \emph{Beam expansion.} The \emph{frontier} (the current set of
424	  partial decompositions being explored, each recorded as a pair
425	  $[\text{primes chosen so far},\ \text{remaining sum}]$) is
426	  expanded for $1$ round (mode \textsf{Fast}) or $3$ rounds
427	  (\textsf{Middle}/\textsf{Deep}), each round multiplying the frontier
428	  size by $n$.
429	
430	\item \emph{Greedy completion.} From every leaf of the beam, a pure
431	  greedy completion is attempted: at each step one subtracts the
432	  largest feasible $1/(p+s)$ until the remainder reaches~$0$.
433	  If the remainder has not reached $0$ after $99$ primes have
434	  been selected, the completion returns \textsc{False} and the
435	  leaf is discarded.
436	  There is no prime-size cutoff: when the unsolved remainder
437	  is a very small rational, the beam candidates are necessarily
438	  huge primes, and the beam expansion (Step~2) returns the
439	  smallest one with no restriction on its size. This is how
440	  primes with hundreds of digits appear in the final set~$P$.
441	
442	\item \emph{Selection.} Among all successful completions, the one
443	  minimising the sum of primes is returned.
444	\end{enumerate}
445	
446	\begin{example}
447	Beam search on target $r = 1/3$, lower bound $b=1$,
448		mode \textsf{Fast}.
449	
450	
451	\textit{Step~1: candidate generation.}
452	List the $n=3$ smallest primes $p > b$ with $1/(p+1) \leq 1/3$,
453	i.e.\ $p \geq 2$:
454	\[
455	  p = 2 \;\bigl({\textstyle\frac{1}{3}}\bigr), \quad
456	  p = 3 \;\bigl({\textstyle\frac{1}{4}}\bigr), \quad
457	  p = 5 \;\bigl({\textstyle\frac{1}{6}}\bigr).
458	\]
459	
460	\textit{Step~2: beam expansion (1 round).}
461	Branch on each candidate and record the remainder:
462	\[
463	\begin{array}{lll}
464	  \text{branch } p=2: & \text{take } \tfrac{1}{3}, &
465	    \text{remainder } = \tfrac{1}{3} - \tfrac{1}{3} = 0
466	    \quad\Rightarrow\quad \text{already complete},\\[4pt]
467	  \text{branch } p=3: & \text{take } \tfrac{1}{4}, &
468	    \text{remainder } = \tfrac{1}{3} - \tfrac{1}{4} = \tfrac{1}{12},\\[4pt]
469	  \text{branch } p=5: & \text{take } \tfrac{1}{6}, &
470	    \text{remainder } = \tfrac{1}{3} - \tfrac{1}{6} = \tfrac{1}{6}.
471	\end{array}
472	\]
473	
474	\textit{Step~3: greedy completion of each leaf.}
475	\begin{itemize}
476	\item Leaf $[2]$: remainder $0$, done. \quad Result: $\{2\}$.
477	\item Leaf $[3]$: remainder $\tfrac{1}{12}$.
478	  Smallest prime $p > 3$ with $\tfrac{1}{p+1} \leq \tfrac{1}{12}$
479	  is $p = 11$; taking it leaves remainder $0$.
480	  Result: $\{3, 11\}$.
481	\item Leaf $[5]$: remainder $\tfrac{1}{6}$.
482	  Take $p=7$ ($\tfrac{1}{8}$), remainder $\tfrac{1}{6}-\tfrac{1}{8}=\tfrac{1}{24}$;
483	  then $p=23$ ($\tfrac{1}{24}$), remainder $0$.
484	  Result: $\{5, 7, 23\}$.
485	\end{itemize}
486	
487	\textit{Step~4: selection.}
488	Among the three completions, the one minimising the sum of primes is chosen:
489	\[
490	  \{2\} \;(\text{sum }= 2), \quad
491	  \{3,11\} \;(\text{sum }= 14), \quad
492	  \{5,7,23\} \;(\text{sum }= 35).
493	\]
494	The beam search returns $\{2\}$, and indeed $\tfrac{1}{2+1} = \tfrac{1}{3}$.
495	Note that all three sets are valid decompositions of $\tfrac{1}{3}$;
496	the beam search finds all of them and picks the simplest.
497	\end{example}
498	
499	\begin{example}[Beam search on target $r = 1/2$, lower bound $b = 6$,
500		mode \textsf{Fast}]
501	This is the sub-problem arising in iteration~3 of Phase~2
502	(elimination of $p^* = 5$, $m = 4$, target $(m-1)/(p^*+1) = 1/2$).
503	The lower bound $b = p^*+1 = 6$ ensures that all replacement primes are strictly greater than $5$.
504	
505	\textit{Step~1: candidate generation.}
506	The $n=3$ smallest primes $p > 5$ with $1/(p+1) \leq 1/2$ are:
507	\[
508	  p = 7 \;\bigl({\textstyle\frac{1}{8}}\bigr), \quad
509	  p = 11 \;\bigl({\textstyle\frac{1}{12}}\bigr), \quad
510	  p = 13 \;\bigl({\textstyle\frac{1}{14}}\bigr).
511	\]
512	
513	\textit{Step~2: beam expansion (1 round).}
514	\[
515	\begin{array}{lll}
516	  \text{branch } p=7{:}  & \text{take } \tfrac{1}{8},  &
517	    \text{remainder} = \tfrac{1}{2}-\tfrac{1}{8} = \tfrac{3}{8},\\[4pt]
518	  \text{branch } p=11{:} & \text{take } \tfrac{1}{12}, &
519	    \text{remainder} = \tfrac{1}{2}-\tfrac{1}{12} = \tfrac{5}{12},\\[4pt]
520	  \text{branch } p=13{:} & \text{take } \tfrac{1}{14}, &
521	    \text{remainder} = \tfrac{1}{2}-\tfrac{1}{14} = \tfrac{3}{7}.
522	\end{array}
523	\]
524	
525	\textit{Step~3: greedy completion of each leaf.}
526	\begin{itemize}
527	\item Leaf $[7]$, remainder $\frac{3}{8}$:
528	  take $p=11$ ($\frac{1}{12}$, rem.\ $\frac{7}{24}$),
529	  $p=13$ ($\frac{1}{14}$, rem.\ $\frac{37}{168}$),
530	  $\ldots$, eventually reaching $p = 2{,}169{,}227$.
531	  Result: $\{7\cm 11\cm 13\cm 17\cm 19\cm 23\cm 29\cm 31\cm 127\cm 1613\cm 2169227\}$,\quad sum of primes $\approx 2.17\times 10^6$.
532	\item Leaf $[11]$, remainder $\frac{5}{12}$:
533	  take $p=13\cm 17\cm 19\cm 23\cm 29\cm 31\cm 37\cm 41\cm 43\cm 47$.
534	  Result: $\{11\cm 13\cm 17\cm 19\cm 23\cm 29\cm 31\cm 37\cm 41\cm 43\cm 47\}$,\quad sum $= 311$.
535	\item Leaf $[13]$, remainder $\frac{3}{7}$:
536	  take $p=17\cm 19\cm 23\cm 29\cm 31\cm 37\cm 41\cm 43\cm 47\cm 53$.
537	  Result: $\{13\cm 17\cm 19\cm 23\cm 29\cm 31\cm 37\cm 41\cm 43\cm 47\cm 53\}$,\quad sum $= 353$.
538	\end{itemize}
539	
540	\textit{Step~4: selection.}
541	The beam selects the completion with the smallest sum of primes,
542	namely leaf $[11]$ with sum $311$.
543	The beam search therefore returns
544	$Q = \{11\cm 13\cm 17\cm 19\cm 23\cm 29\cm 31\cm 37\cm 41\cm 43\cm 47\}$, and indeed
545	\[
546	  \sum_{p \in Q} \frac{1}{p+1}
547	  = \frac{1}{12}+\frac{1}{14}+\frac{1}{18}+\frac{1}{20}+\frac{1}{24}
548	  +\frac{1}{30}+\frac{1}{32}+\frac{1}{38}+\frac{1}{42}+\frac{1}{44}+\frac{1}{48}
549	  = \frac{1}{2}.
550	\]
551	Comparing the three leaves, leaf $[7]$ produces a prime with more than
552	six decimal digits — illustrating why a pure greedy search (always
553	taking the largest possible term) is impractical.
554	The beam search, by exploring multiple branches simultaneously, finds
555	the much cleaner solution from leaf $[11]$.
556	\end{example}
557	
558	\begin{example}
559	[Splitting table + beam search, target $r = 1/2$,
560		lower bound $b = 6$, mode \textsf{Middle}]
561		\label{ex:split}
562	
563	This is the sub-problem arising in iteration~3 of Phase~2
564	(elimination of $p^* = 5$, $m = 4$, replacement target
565	$(m-1)/(p^*+1) = 3/6 = 1/2$, lower bound $b = p^*+1 = 6$).
566	The actual run uses mode \textsf{Middle} (since \texttt{prefer\_large=True}).
567	
568	
569	The function \texttt{\_decompose\_via\_split\_table} iterates through
570	the \textsf{Middle} table ($82$ entries) until one fully succeeds.
571	In our example, the first successful entry is Entry~$1$,
572	the pair $[1/4,\,3/4]$, which splits the target $1/2$ into two
573	independent sub-problems:
574	\[
575	  \frac{1}{4}\cdot\frac{1}{2} \;=\; \frac{1}{8},
576	  \qquad
577	  \frac{3}{4}\cdot\frac{1}{2} \;=\; \frac{3}{8}.
578	\]
579	
580	\textit{Beam on $1/8$, lower bound $6$.}
581	The unique prime $p > 5$ with $1/(p+1) = 1/8$ is $p = 7$.
582	The beam returns $\{7\}$ immediately.
583	
584	\textit{Beam on $3/8$, lower bound $6$.}
585	The beam (with $n=4$ candidates in \textsf{Middle} mode, and $3$
586	expansion rounds) returns $\{7, 11, 19, 23, 29, 31, 97, 4703\}$.
587	Indeed,
588	\[
589	  \frac{1}{8}+\frac{1}{12}+\frac{1}{20}+\frac{1}{24}
590	  +\frac{1}{30}+\frac{1}{32}+\frac{1}{98}+\frac{1}{4704}
591	  \;=\; \frac{3}{8}.
592	\]
593	
594	\textit{Combined result.}
595	Concatenating the two sub-results gives
596	$Q = \{7\cm 7\cm 11\cm 19\cm 23\cm 29\cm 31\cm 97\cm 4703\}$, and indeed
597	\[
598	  \frac{1}{8}+\frac{1}{8}+\frac{1}{12}+\frac{1}{20}+\frac{1}{24}
599	  +\frac{1}{30}+\frac{1}{32}+\frac{1}{98}+\frac{1}{4704}
600	  \;=\; \frac{1}{2}.
601	\]
602	The prime $7$ appears twice because both sub-problems independently
603	selected it as their first term.
604	This is consistent with what was reported in iteration~3: the two
605	sub-problems are solved in isolation, without any global check on
606	which primes are already present, so new multiplicities can be
607	introduced — which is precisely why $\ct$ rises to $9$ after
608	this iteration.
609	
610	\end{example}
611	
612	\subsection{Phase~1: initial multiset $M_0$}\label{sec:phase1}
613	
614	In Phase~1 we apply the beam search above to the target rational $r$
615	in mode \textsf{Fast} (then \textsf{Middle}, then \textsf{Deep} on
616	failure). 
617	
618	\begin{example}[Phase~1 for the run $(r=2,\,s=+1)$]\label{ex:phase1}
619	The beam search is called on target $r=2$ in mode \textsf{Fast}.
620	The function \texttt{\_decompose\_via\_split\_table} tries table
621	entries in order; the first one that fully succeeds is the pair
622	$[2/5,\,3/5]$, which splits the target into two independent
623	sub-problems:
624	\[
625	  \frac{2}{5}\cdot 2 \;=\; \frac{4}{5},
626	  \qquad
627	  \frac{3}{5}\cdot 2 \;=\; \frac{6}{5}.
628	\]
629	The beam solves each independently:
630	\[
631	  \frac{4}{5} \;\longrightarrow\; \{2,3,5,19\}, \qquad
632	  \frac{6}{5} \;\longrightarrow\; \{2,3,5,7,11,13,17,19,23,43,3467,4005539\}.
633	\]
634	Concatenating the two sub-results gives the initial multiset
635	\[
636	  M_0^+ \;=\; \{\,2,2,3,3,5,5,7,11,13,17,19,19,23,43,3467,4005539\,\},
637	\]
638	of size $16$, with $\ct(M_0^+)=4$. The four repeated primes are
639	$2$, $3$, $5$, and $19$, each appearing exactly twice, one copy
640	coming from each sub-result. One verifies in exact rational arithmetic that
641	\[
642	  \sum_{p\,\in\,M_0^+} \frac{1}{p+1}
643	  \;=\;
644	  \frac{2}{3}+\frac{2}{4}+\frac{2}{6}
645	  +\frac{1}{8}+\frac{1}{12}+\frac{1}{14}+\frac{1}{18}
646	  +\frac{2}{20}+\frac{1}{24}+\frac{1}{44}
647	  +\frac{1}{3468}+\frac{1}{4005540}
648	  \;=\; 2.
649	\]
650	Phase~1 terminates here and hands $M_0^+$ to Phase~2,
651	which will iteratively eliminate the four repeated primes.
652	\end{example}
653	
654	\subsection{Phase~2: deduplication loop}\label{sec:phase2}
655	
656	Let $M$ denote the current multiset, initialised to $M = M_0$. Each
657	iteration of Phase~2 performs the following step.
658	
659	\begin{enumerate}
660	\item \emph{Pick a repeated prime.} Let $p^* = \min\{p \in M : \mu_M(p) \geq 2\}$
661	  and let $m = \mu_M(p^*)$.
662	\item \emph{Replace.} Find a list $Q$ of distinct primes, all $> p^*$,
663	  with 
664	  \[
665	    \sum_{q \in Q} \frac{1}{q+s} = \frac{m-1}{p^*+s}.
666	  \]
667	  This is done by calling the beam search of Section~\ref{sec:beam}
668	  on the target $(m-1)/(p^*+s)$ with lower bound $p^*$, with each
669	  splitting table entry tried in turn.
670	\item \emph{Update.} Remove $m-1$ copies of $p^*$ from $M$, and
671	  add $Q$. The new multiset $M'$ satisfies
672	  $S^{(s)}(M') = S^{(s)}(M) = r$.
673	\end{enumerate}
674	
675	See Section~\ref{sec:example} for an illustration of how this works.
676	
677	\subsection{Termination}\label{sec:traj}
678	
679	
680	A naive expectation is that $\ct$ should decrease monotonically.
681	This is \emph{not} the case: the replacement of $m-1$ copies of $p^*$
682	by a list $Q$ of new primes may introduce \emph{new} multiplicities,
683	because some primes in $Q$ may already lie in $M$. Thus $\ct$ can rise
684	sharply in the early iterations.
685	
686	As shown in the two figures of Section~\ref{sec:proof}, the trajectory
687	of $\ct$ in practice follows a roughly unimodal shape: it first rises
688	(with occasional downward fluctuations) to a peak, then decreases
689	(with occasional upward fluctuations) to $0$. This behaviour is not
690	proved mathematically, but can be intuitively understood as follows.
691	In the early iterations, the repeated primes are small and their
692	replacements $Q$ frequently collide with primes already in $M$,
693	causing $\ct$ to grow. As the frontier of unresolved primes moves
694	upward, collisions become rarer and $\ct$ begins its descent.
695	
696	The algorithm terminates successfully if and only if $\ct$ eventually
697	reaches $0$. Guaranteeing termination for all inputs $r$ would provide
698	a fully constructive proof of Theorems~\ref{conj:sun_plus}
699	and~\ref{conj:sun_minus}, complementing Bloom's existential argument.
700	This remains open: we can only verify termination experimentally for
701	specific inputs, as reported in Section~\ref{sec:proof}.
702	
703	
704	
705	\section{Concrete examples from the run $(r=2,\,s=+1)$}\label{sec:example}
706	From Example~\ref{ex:phase1},
707	the initial multiset is
708	\[
709	M_0^+ = \{\,2,2,3,3,5,5,7,11,13,17,19,19,23,43,3467,4005539\,\},
710	\]
711	with $\ct(M_0^+)=4$, the four repeated primes being $2,3,5,19$.
712	
713	\medskip
714	\noindent\textit{Iteration 1: $p^*=2$, $m=2$.}
715	Phase~2 removes one copy of $2$ and must find a set $Q$
716	of distinct primes $>2$ satisfying
717	\[
718	  \sum_{q\in Q}\frac{1}{q+1} \;=\; \frac{m-1}{p^*+1} \;=\; \frac{1}{3}.
719	\]
720	The beam search, using the table entry $[1/3,\,2/3]$ to split the
721	target $1/3$ into sub-targets $1/9$ and $2/9$, returns
722	$Q=\{5,7,23\}$.  Indeed,
723	\[
724	  \frac{1}{6}+\frac{1}{8}+\frac{1}{24} \;=\; \frac{1}{3}.
725	\]
726	The new multiset is
727	\[
728	  M_1 = \{\,2,3,3,5,5,5,7,7,11,13,17,19,19,23,23,43,3467,4005539\,\}.
729	\]
730	Since $5$, $7$, $23$ were already in $M_0^+$, their multiplicities
731	increase, and $\ct$ rises from $4$ to $6$.
732	The repeated primes are now $\{3,5,7,19,23\}$
733	with multiplicities $\{2,3,2,2,2\}$.
734	
735	\medskip
736	\noindent\textit{Iteration 2: $p^*=3$, $m=2$.}
737	One copy of $3$ is removed; the replacement target is
738	\[
739	  \frac{m-1}{p^*+1} \;=\; \frac{1}{4}.
740	\]
741	The beam search returns $Q=\{5,11\}$. Indeed,
742	\[
743	  \frac{1}{6}+\frac{1}{12} \;=\; \frac{1}{4}.
744	\]
745	The new multiset is
746	\[
747	  M_2 = \{\,2,3,5,5,5,5,7,7,11,11,13,17,19,19,23,23,43,3467,4005539\,\}.
748	\]
749	Adding $5$ and $11$ (both already present) raises $\ct$ further to $7$.
750	The prime $5$ now has multiplicity $4$.
751	
752	\medskip
753	\noindent\textit{Iteration 3: $p^*=5$, $m=4$.}
754	The multiplicity of $5$ grew from $2$ in $M_0^+$ to $4$ in $M_2$
755	because iterations~1 and~2 each introduced an extra copy of $5$.
756	Three copies of $5$ are removed, leaving one.
757	The replacement target is
758	\[
759	  \frac{m-1}{p^*+1} \;=\; \frac{3}{6} \;=\; \frac{1}{2}.
760	\]
761	A larger target is not a problem: it simply means the beam search
762	must find more primes to fill it.
763	From Example~\ref{ex:split}, the beam search returns the multiset
764	$Q=\{7\cm 7\cm 11\cm 19\cm 23\cm 29\cm 31\cm 97\cm 4703\}$
765	(nine primes, with $7$ appearing twice). Indeed,
766	\[
767	  \frac{1}{8}+\frac{1}{8}+\frac{1}{12}+\frac{1}{20}+\frac{1}{24}
768	  +\frac{1}{30}+\frac{1}{32}+\frac{1}{98}+\frac{1}{4704}
769	  \;=\; \frac{1}{2}.
770	\]
771	Since five of the nine primes in $Q$ (namely $7,7,11,19,23$)
772	are already present in $M_2$, the count rises further:
773	$\ct(M_3) = 9$.
774	The repeated primes in $M_3$ are $\{7,11,19,23\}$
775	with multiplicities $\{4,3,3,3\}$.
776	
777	\medskip
778	This illustrates why $\ct$ grows during the early iterations:
779	each replacement introduces primes that are already in the multiset,
780	creating new collisions faster than old ones are resolved.
781	Although $\ct$ has increased at every step so far, the algorithm
782	is making irreversible progress:
783	the primes $2$, $3$, and $5$ will never be repeated again in any
784	subsequent multiset $M_j$. The frontier of unresolved primes moves
785	strictly upward at every iteration.
786	
787	
788	
789	
790	% -------------------------------------------------------------------
791	\section{The two runs: proofs of Theorems~\ref{thm:main_plus} and~\ref{thm:main_minus}}\label{sec:proof}
792	% -------------------------------------------------------------------
793	
794	The two theorems are obtained by running \texttt{sunconj.py} with
795	different command-line arguments. We summarise both runs in
796	Table~\ref{tab:summary}, then comment on the trajectory of $\ct$ in
797	each case.
798	
799	\begin{table}[h]
800	\centering
801	\begin{tabular}{l c c}
802	\hline
803	& Theorem~\ref{thm:main_plus}: $r=2$, $s=+1$
804	& Theorem~\ref{thm:main_minus}: $r=3$, $s=-1$ \\
805	\hline
806	Command          & \texttt{sunconj.py +1 2 false true} & \texttt{sunconj.py -1 3 false true} \\
807	$|M_0|$          & $16$                & $12$ \\
808	$\ct(M_0)$       & $4$                 & $2$ \\
809	Phase~2 iterations & $452$             & $167$ \\
810	Peak $\ct$       & $180$ at $j=191$    & $57$ at $j=48$ \\
811	$|P|$            & $1963$              & $698$ \\
812	Largest prime     & $181$ digits       & $68$ digits \\
813	\hline
814	\end{tabular}
815	\caption{Summary of the two runs of \texttt{sunconj.py}}\label{tab:summary}
816	\end{table}
817	
818	\subsection{Proof of Theorem~\ref{thm:main_plus}: $r = 2$ via $1/(p+1)$}\label{sec:proof_plus}
819	
820	Run the program \texttt{sunconj.py} on the target $r = 2$ with the
821	command
822	\begin{verbatim}
823	  python sunconj.py +1 2 false true
824	\end{verbatim}
825	From Example~\ref{ex:phase1},
826	Phase~1 produces the multiset
827	\begin{equation}\label{eq:M0_plus}
828	  M_0^{+} \;=\; \{\,2,2,3,3,5,5,7,11,13,17,19,19,23,43,3467,4005539\,\},
829	\end{equation}
830	of size $16$, with $\ct(M_0^{+}) = 4$ (four excess copies, namely the
831	second copies of $2$, $3$, $5$, and $19$). 
832	
833	Phase~2 then runs for $452$ iterations. The trajectory of $\ct$ is
834	plotted in Figure~\ref{fig:traj_plus}. 
835	It is roughly unimodal: $\ct$ rises
836	(with occasional downward fluctuations) from $4$ at iteration $0$
837	to a peak of $180$ at iteration $191$, then decreases
838	(with occasional upward fluctuations) to $0$ at iteration $451$.
839

870	
871	
872	After $452$ iterations the program outputs a line
873	\texttt{RESGOOD~$2$~[2,3,5,\ldots]~1963}, 
874	which lists the elements of
875	the set
876	\[
877	  P_+ \;=\; \{p_1, p_2, \ldots, p_{1963}\} \;\subset\; \PP
878	\]
879	in increasing order. The full list is contained in the supplementary
880	file 
881	\texttt{prime\_set\_P\_\allowbreak plus2.txt}. 
882	We verify three properties:
883	\begin{enumerate}
884	\item \emph{All elements are prime.} 
885	
886	\item \emph{All elements are distinct.} The output list, sorted in
887	  increasing order, has length $1963$. 
888	
889	\item \emph{The sum equals $2$ exactly.} The sum
890	  $\sum_{p \in P_+} 1/(p+1)=2$ is computed in arbitrary-precision rational
891	  arithmetic.
892	\end{enumerate}
893	The smallest element of $P_+$ is $2$, the largest is a prime with $181$
894	decimal digits. This proves Theorem~\ref{thm:main_plus}. \hfill$\Box$
895	
896	\subsection{Proof of Theorem~\ref{thm:main_minus}: $r = 3$ via $1/(p-1)$}\label{sec:proof_minus}
897	
898	Run the program with the command
899	\begin{verbatim}
900	  python sunconj.py -1 3 false true
901	\end{verbatim}
902	($s=-1$, target $r=3$). Phase~1 produces the initial multiset
903	\begin{equation}\label{eq:M0_minus}
904	  M_0^{-} \;=\; \{\,2, 3, 3, 5, 5, 7, 11, 13, 17, 19, 37, 241\,\},
905	\end{equation}
906	of size $12$, with $\ct(M_0^{-}) = 2$ (two excess copies, namely the
907	second copies of $3$ and $5$). One checks directly in exact rational
908	arithmetic that
909	\[
910	  S^{(-1)}(M_0^{-})
911	  \;=\; \frac{1}{1} + \frac{2}{2}  + \frac{2}{4} 
912	        + \frac{1}{6} + \frac{1}{10} + \frac{1}{12} + \frac{1}{16}
913	        + \frac{1}{18} + \frac{1}{36} + \frac{1}{240}
914	  \;=\; 3.
915	\]
916	Phase~2 runs for only $167$ iterations (compared to $452$ for the
917	$s=+1$ case), peaking at $\ct = 57$ at $j = 48$ and then decreasing to
918	$0$ at $j=166$. The trajectory is plotted in Figure~\ref{fig:traj_minus}.
919

950	
951	The qualitative shape of the trajectory is the same as in
952	Figure~\ref{fig:traj_plus}: a climb, a noisy plateau, and a monotone
953	descent.
954	
955	After $167$ iterations the program outputs a line
956	\texttt{RESGOOD~$3$~[2,3,5,\ldots]~698}, which lists the elements of
957	the set
958	\[
959	  P_- \;=\; \{q_1, q_2, \ldots, q_{698}\} \;\subset\; \PP
960	\]
961	in increasing order. The full list is contained in the supplementary
962	file 
963	\texttt{prime\_set\_P\_\allowbreak minus3.txt}. 
964	The same three verifications
965	as in Section~\ref{sec:proof_plus} are performed:
966	\begin{enumerate}
967	\item all $698$ elements are prime;
968	\item the list, sorted in increasing order, has length $698$;
969	\item the sum $\sum_{p \in P_-} 1/(p-1)=3$
970	  exactly.
971	\end{enumerate}
972	The smallest element of $P_-$ is $2$, and the largest is a prime with $68$ decimal digits. This proves
973	Theorem~\ref{thm:main_minus}. \hfill$\Box$
974	
975	\subsection{Remarks on the two sets}
976	
977	\begin{remark}
978	The sets $P_+$ and $P_-$ produced by our algorithm are not claimed to
979	be optimal in any sense. Their cardinalities depend on the splitting
980	tables and on the choice of beam-search candidates; smaller sets
981	certainly exist. Finding the minimum $|P|$ such that
982	$\sum_{p \in P} 1/(p+s) = r$ is a natural open question for each
983	given pair $(r, s)$.
984	\end{remark}
985	
986	\begin{remark}
987	The largest primes in $P_+$ and $P_-$ have $181$ and $68$ decimal
988	digits respectively. Such large primes are not surprising: the
989	identity~\eqref{eq:split} roughly squares the denominator at each
990	splitting step, and several chained splittings during Phase~2 can
991	compound this growth.
992	\end{remark}
993

160	$\sum_{p \in P} 1/(p+s) = r$ is produced and verified in exact rational
161	arithmetic. Moreover, the produced sets are short: for every $r$ in each
162	family, the number of primes $|P|$ satisfies the bound collected in
163	Table~\ref{tab:bounds}. 
164	\end{theorem}
165	
166	\begin{table}[h]
167	\centering
168	\begin{tabular}{l c c}
169	\hline
170	Family & shift $s$ & bound on $|P|$ \\
171	\hline
172	Fractions $a/b$, $1\le a<b\le 1000$   & $\pm 1$ & $b+8$ \\
173	Prime reciprocals $1/q$, $q\le 10^5$  & $\pm 1$ & $\log_2 q + 1$ \\
174	Power reciprocals $1/2^k$, $k\le 40$  & $\pm 1$ & $k+1$ \\
175	\hline
176	\end{tabular}
177	\vspace{1ex}
178	\caption{Length bounds for the produced sets $P$}
179	\label{tab:bounds}
180	\end{table}
181	%
182	The bounds in Table~\ref{tab:bounds} are established by direct verification
183	over the finite families; they are properties of the particular sets $P$
184	produced by \texttt{sunconj.py}, not of every possible decomposition. 
185	
186	We observe that  the reciprocal families~(2) and~(3)    obey the very same logarithmic bound, which
187	suggests that it may hold for
188	the reciprocal of an arbitrary integer, as stated in next conjecture.
189	
190	
191	\begin{conjecture}\label{conj:reciprocal}
192	Let $d \ge 2$ be an integer and let $s \in \{+1, -1\}$. Then $1/d$ admits a
193	prime decomposition
194	\[
195	  \sum_{p \in P} \frac{1}{p+s} \;=\; \frac{1}{d}
196	  \qquad (P \subset \PP \text{ a finite set of distinct primes})
197	\]
198	whose length satisfies
199	\[
200	  |P| \;\le\; \log_2 d + 1 .
201	\]
202	\end{conjecture}
203	
204	The existence of \emph{some} such decomposition is already guaranteed by
205	Bloom's Theorems~\ref{conj:sun_plus} and~\ref{conj:sun_minus}; the content of
206	Conjecture~\ref{conj:reciprocal} lies entirely in the \emph{length} bound.
207	Bloom's proof, however, settles existence non-constructively and gives no
208	control on the number of terms.  The logarithmic
209	bound $|P| \le \log_2 d + 1$ is therefore beyond the reach of~\cite{Bloom},
210	and appears to be a genuinely constructive, algorithmic phenomenon.
211	
212	We have verified Conjecture~\ref{conj:reciprocal} for both shifts and for
213	every integer $2 \le d \le 10\,000$, as well as for all primes $d \le 10^5$
214	and all powers $d = 2^k$ with $k \le 40$ (Table~\ref{tab:bounds}); the check
215	for arbitrary $d$ is carried out by the self-contained program
216	\texttt{sunconj\_reciprocal.py}. 
217	
218	We deliberately do not conjecture a companion bound on the largest prime
219	$\max P$: empirically its exponent $\log_d(\max P)$ is erratic and grows with
220	$d$, so no fixed power of $d$ appears to bound it for all reciprocals.
221	
222	\medskip
223	
224	The proofs are constructive and use exactly the same algorithm, run
225	with different inputs: the sets $P_+$ and $P_-$ are produced by the
226	program \texttt{sunconj.py} described in Section~\ref{sec:algo} and
227	listed in full in the supplementary files \texttt{prime\_set\_P\_plus2.txt}
228	and \texttt{prime\_set\_P\_minus3.txt}. All identities are verified
229	in arbitrary-precision rational arithmetic (\texttt{gmpy2.mpq}).
230	All programs and data files are available in a permanent public
231	repository, allowing independent verification of the computations:
232	\begin{center}
233	\url{https://github.com/GuoniuHan/sunconj}
234	\end{center}

Added to `response.txt` — a short **"Additional change"** paragraph after the Comment 3 response:

> Motivated by the computations, the revised version also includes a new conjecture (Conjecture 7): for every integer `d ≥ 2` and shift `s ∈ {+1,−1}`, `1/d` admits a shifted-prime decomposition of length at most `log₂(d) + 1`. This quantitative bound goes beyond Bloom's (non-constructive, term-count-uncontrolled) existence theorem. The conjecture is now also mentioned in the abstract.
